There seems to be even less
than the usual excuse for the dis
astrous tram wreck on the South- i
crn Pacific on Monday morning.
A special, carrying delegates io
the meeting of the National Edu
cational Association, sent out by i
the Burlington rjad, but having i
the right of way, crashed into a
freight train on the main line at a
regular station â a train which by
all the rules of railroading, and
apparently by its orders, should
.have been safe on a siding ten
m.les away. This was a gross
blunder, in giving or in receiving
orders.
Worse even than this, in making
up the special the Southern Pacific
opera: ing department violated the
oldest rule of railroading by put
ting a light tourist sleeper next
to the engine without the custom- j
ary protection of a baggage car
to shield the sleeper in case of
just such an accident as this.
When the collision came, death to
some of those in the sleeper was
inevitable. The tender of the
locomotive, with the force of the
whole momentum of the moving
tram, was driven back through the
tourist car. It is little short of
marvelous that there were no
more latahties.

and the rescuers went to work to pet

Axes were used on the top of the
sleeper nearest the tender and the
searchers came upon Miss White,
daughter of Mrs. Lena Thomas, and
Miss May Oliver, both of whom were
wedged in between the tender and the f
roof of their car. Th< y were extricated j

with a saw.
In lower 13 and 14 the rescuers r-ame
upon the bodies of Mrs. Thomas and j
Miss Harris, a young teacher, dead.
but not held by any of the crushed
timbers. They were taken out and sent |
tip the undertaking parlors of .Charles
Schank. An examination of Mrs. '
Thomas showed that her neck had been
broken by the end of the tender being
forced Into the berth. Miss Harris, it
is believed, died of fright or by smoke :
asphyxiation. Her face was lacerated j
but not seriously. She was 30 years of
age and Mrs. Thomas about .". The in
jured people were taken to the Russ
House, where they were cared for by
the railroad's surgeons; T>r. Armistead
and I" - . I >owle and Stratton.
While all 6f the unfortunates com- j
plained of various pains and aches. the
physicians state they could not find any
very serious Injuries, bruises, ]â¢â â 
tions and sprains being the majority of
the ailments.
It was daylight before the flames
were extinguished and not till they had
devoured two cars and all the wood
work on the engine.
Of the accident but one story is told
here. The special was coming up the
valley at the rate of forty miles an
hour. Opposite the station on the main
track stood the combination freight
train, its engine at the water tank.
Only six minutes had elapsed since it
had stopped. The station signal light
showed the white, which means "clear
track." The engineer <>f the special
gave the customary two long and two
short blasts of the whistle, and seeing
the white light kept right on. Sudden
ly he epied the stationary train, the air
brakes were applied, the lever was re
versed, but the speed was so great that
nothing could stop the special and a
crash followed.
The special's engine ploughed half
way through the freight train's last car
and the force behind lifted the engine
and hurled it over on its side.
Engineer R. B. Allen and Fireman
W. P. Creigh at this moment jumped
from their stations and wore uninjured.
T'le condition of the station lights is
en using mm h comment. Several of
those who were out at the time of Wi*
collision state that when the freight '
train pulled in it was given the white j
light on the main track to indicate that
everything was clear on these rails.
When the special's whistle for sig
nals was heard a glance In the direc
tion of the station showed that the !
white light for an open and clear track |
still shone brightly, and Watchman J.
H. Madden remarked:
"'I knew that those two trains were j
going to get mixed up and somebody
get hurt."
Station Master Edgar Hartwell was
questioned as to the condition of these
signal lights, but ho declined to discuss
the accident or any of Its feature*. He
' said:
"The station signals were out correct,
and if there is any blame it lies with
those In charge of the trains. My re
port will be made to my superiors and
to no one else."
He declined to even state whether or j
not the red light should have been dis
played with a freight train on the main
track and a through special due at any
moment.
If the story told by Ben Livingston, j
a plumber, and Frank Homer, be true, I
some one disobeyed or failed to under
stand orders. Both reached the wreck
soon after the crash, and near the over
turned engine one found a paper, the
order to the engineer of the excursion
train. In substance it was an instruc
tion for him to pass the freight train
at Inpomar, eight or nine miles ahead,
the freight train being instructed to.go
upon a siding so as to let the special
pass. The same order, so the men say,
mentioned other trains to be passed
during the night and where they would
turn out t" let the excursion train
go by.
Railroad men subsequently got hold
of the engineer's orders.
One of the most remarkable escapes
from death among the many was that
of Calvin Bullock, the porter. He
I went to the front platform for a
breath of fresh air and was obliged to
grasp the railing when the air brakes
were put on. The shock of the first
impact threw " < porter clear off the
cars and high into the air. He landed
all in a heap just outside of the danger
line and sustained only a few sprains
and bruises.
The Coroner came from Modesto and
had the bodies of the victims viewed by
a jury. The inquest will be held on
Moi day at 3 o'clock in the afternoon.
The Injured left for San Francisco.

of the company. Drs. Lame and Armsiead
of Newman accompanied them. They were

the hotel office was crowded with them
Belvea and their belongings.
It did not take the knowledge that tho
part} had been wrecked to make it plain
that" its members had been through a
terrible experience. Not one of them that
did not look tired and worn out, the ladies
particularly showing the effects of their
thrilling night experience. None of them
were badly Injured, but all were more
or less bruised and suffered from
shock. One or two of the ladies were
swathed In bandages and all were cov
ered with the marks of travel. Several
I ladies, who bad lost part of their cloth

All stories of the survivors agree that,
after the first confusion following the
crash and the extinguishing of the lights,
there was. not the slightest panic in the
coach. The men behaved like heroes and
th<-> ladies were self-possessed and helpful.
They ;Ui got into what clothing they could
find In the darkness, intensified by the
striking of matches, and sloshed around
in the water and out on the earth as
quickly as possible, the blaze from the
fiercely burning cars of the freight train
showing them the awfulness of the death
they had s<> narrowly and, for most of
them, so cheaply escaped.
The passengers of the wrecked sleeper,
who imagined a moment before that they
had crashed through a bridge and were
about to be drowned in the waters of a
river, received a second shock hefore they
hardly had time to feel themselves on the
solid earth. The Fire Department of
Newman, with the help of the. oc
cupants of the other coaches of the spe
cial, was busily engaged in stopping the
progress of the flames in the wreck, when
the alarm was passed around that an
"il tank on a fiatcar on another track
within a few feet of the burning locomo
tive was full of coal oil and was likely
to explode at any moment. With one ae
e.ird the scantily clad passengers ran na
far as they could to some place where
iheir lives would be sate, if only for a
moment till they could catch their
breath. Agents and physicians of the
railroad arrived on the scene in a short
time and soon were busy caring for the
passengers until the wrecking train
should arrive. The survivors of the
wrecked coach were taken to the Russ
House at Newman, where physicians
made a hasty examination of their inju
ries, while others assisted the firemen to
cut out from the wreckage the bodies of.
Mrs. Thomas and Miss Harris.
When the wrecking train arrived from
Oakland, another train was made up for
the city and the passengers were given
the option of coming here and being sent
to Los Angeles or going on the eight un
injured cars of the ill-fated special. All

MONACO REFUSES TO
FIGHT COSTELLANE
The PriQce Declares That Aqqo
Gould's Husband Is a Bogus
Count.
NEW YORK, July 10â A Journal rable from Paris says: The Prince
of Monaco has declined to fight a duel with Count Bonl de Oastellane on
account of the Dreyfus case. In his reply to Castellane's challenge, the
Prince of Monaco openly gives as his reason for the declination the fact
that Castellane Is beneath his station. He says that he has reason to
believe that Castellane has assumed the title of Count when he is not
entitled to it. Monaco says that under the circumstances the challenge
from Castellane is an Impertinence, and is not entitled to notice.
The challenge grew out of the active sympathy for Dreyfus and Mm**.
Dreyfus on the part of the Prince of Monaco, which greatly incensed
Castellane. After Monaco had writfpn Mme. Dreyfus and invited her
husband to visit his palace, the royalist husband of Anna Gould openly
insulted the Prince.
The Sleeks one of the leading Dreyfus organs, has taken the matter
up, and is conducting a severe personal campaign against the Count,
whom it accuses of being a bogus count. It has announced its intention
of soon publishing a biography of Jay Gould, the father of the Countess.
ROYALISTS MIXED UP
IN THE DREYFUS CASE.
Special Cable to The Call and the New York Herald. Copy
right, 1899, by James Gordon Bennett.
PARIS. July 10.â M. Andre Buffet, a representative of the Due d'Or
leans' political bureau, was interviewed by the Figaro in regard to the report
which the prefect of police presented to the Government on the Orleanist
party, and which La Gazette de France, the Royalist organ, published yes
terday. The report says the League of ' Patriots and Anti-Semite League
were in communication with the Duke of Orleans, who, it is said, sent 5000
francs to M. Deroulede, and other large sums to Jules Guerin. Altogether
the Royalist party is said to have spent 300,000 francs in organizing the at
tempt at a coup d'etat at the military barracks. M. Buffet says: "The best
thing we could do in reply to recent reports of the plot was to show up
what our adversaries are. We therefore published the principal document of
the prosecution with which we were threatenedâ a report of the prefect of
police â and now let them prosecute us."
In regard to the truth or otherwise of the report, M. Buffet refused to
reply to many statements made. However, he declared them inexact; for
instance, those regarding money alleged to have been given to MM. De
roulede and Guerin. M. Buffet further denied that he was the representa
tive in France of the Due d'Orleans. M. de Haussonville was the repre
sentative of the Oomte de Paris. He resigned on 'the death of that Prince,
and since then the Due d'Orleans desired no further representatives.
M. Buffet is the representative of his political bureau. His royal high
ness wishes to have supreme control of his party and employs various per
sons according to their aptitude.
As to the authenticity of the report on that point there is no doubt. La
Gazette de France obtained it by perfectly legal mean?. It formed part of
the dossier in the recent persecution of the Jeunesse Royaliste. As to the
society, "Sapher Royal," M. Buffet declared that until he road its name in
M. Blanc's report he had never heard the name. As for the "Alouette Gau
loise." it is a Royalist group at La Villette, and as to the existence of
which no mystery was ever made. "Gamelle" is probably a Royalist meet
ing held at Durand's restaurant under the name of Poule au Pot. Another
Vrror in the report is regarding M. de Plas, the administrator of the Ga
zette de France. Everybody knows M. Janicot is the sole proprietor and di
rector of that journal. Everybody knows, too, that the Due d'Andiffret-
Pasquier gave his resignation to the Comte de Paris long before M. Du
fenille, who gave his to the Due d'Orleans.
"You can think what you like." added M. Buffet. "It would really be a
pity to reply to the ensemble of the report and then prosecute us, and we
will reply. For the moment I know nothing."
It is rumored that as a result of the revelation of the Inhuman cruelty
practiced upon Dreyfus while on Devils Island, M. le Bon, the Minister who
was responsible for the orders sent to M. Deniel, while in charge of the
penal settlement where Dreyfus was confined, will be proceeded against,
the Government not confining its action to yesterday's dismissal of M. De
niel. M. Bertillon, the handwriting expert who testified before the <'ourt of
Cassation that Dreyfus was the author of the bordereau, still remains at the
head of the anthroi.ometric department of the Prefecture or Police, but has
boen deprived of his official duties.
Baron Baulny, another of the aristocratic promoters of the demonstra
tion against President Loubet at Auteuil, has been pardoned. It is under
stood that M. Loubet also desired to pardon Baron Christiana, who made
the assault upon him, but the Cabinet decided that this was quite out of the
question.

INTENSE EXCITEMENT
AMONG WE DELEGATES
Teachers at Lrs Anje/es Anxous
About Friends on the
' Wrecked Tram.
LOS ANGELES, July 10.â When the
news was received to-day that a wreck
had occurred on the Southern Pacific, and
that Bfcveral teachers have been killed and
wounded, there was intense excitement
amon? the visiting delegates. Many had
friends on the train, and they besieged
the general headquarters of the National
Educational Association for particulars
of the wreck and the names of the dead
and injured.
Report* at first were grossly exagger
ated jss to the extent of the accident, and
when it was learned that the fatalities
were L'mited the excitement subsided, but
the news, nevertheless, cast a gloom over
the visitors. Ther^ were many expresions
of lndigration at such an aparently inex
cusable accident, and it seems probable,
that action may be taken by the National
Association or the board of directors of
that body.
Secretary Irvin Shepard. when asked
about the matter to-night, said: "The
board or directors of the National Edu
cational Association meets to-morrow
morning at 10 o'clock. The directors may
then take some step in the matter, but I
am lot now prepared to say what, and
would dislike to make any statement in
anticipation of their possible action."
Dr. C. L. Howard of the Columbia
School, St. Louis, spoke this evening in
eulogistic terms of Miss Addie Harris,
one of the ladies killed in the collision at
N<wman this morning. The deceased was
about 30 yeara of age and was second as
sistant at the Madison School, St. Louis.
Edwin D. Luckey, who was one of i.ie
Injured, was the principal of the Ellerds
ville School, St. Louis, and was in charge
of the party.
LAST LETTERS SENT
BY COUNTESS HASSALL
Tell a Pitiful Story of a Proud
Woman's Struggle to Obtain
a Living.
STOCKTON, July 10.â The last letters
written by Lillian (Countess) Hassall, the
aristocratic English woman who shot her
friend. Mr?. Will Ilickman. and herself
on May 1, disclose a life filled with sad
romance. Mrs. Hassall was seen to mail
some letters a few hours before the trag
edy. Until to-day all efforts on the part
of those interested to locate the recipients
failed. One of these letters went to Mips
Edith Cox. who received it in Oakland nt
2 o'clock the following day. It was this
young woman to whom she left the rem
nants of her once magnificent wardrobe.
Across the first page of the letter is
written. "Silence if golden." The writer
reminds her friend that she had promised
to let her know if anything unusual took
place, and says:
"I am determined to end this wretched
struggle for existence."
It recites her disappointment at some
length. Portions of the letter were with
held, but the recipient's comments there
on are sufficient to Justify the presump
tion that Mrs. Hassall wrote the letters
I just before the tragedy, and consequently
it follows that when the two women went
I to their room both had determined to end
their lives.
Miss Cox has a series of letters telling
j of a proud woman's struggle- with hunger
j and privation. On February 2 "the Count
ess" thanked her for the loan of |3. and
i toid how she had been selling her silver
ware to buy food. March 15 she declared,
"Thank God, I have work at last. Only
$3 a week, but enough to buy food."
The letters indicate that Mrs. Hassall's
character was altogether different from
j that pictured at th* time of the double
murder.
REVIEWING THE CASE
OF CAPTAIN CARTER
Mr. McKinley Said to Have Asked
the Attorney General for an
Early Report.
NEW YORK, July 10.â The Sun's special
from Washington says: The case of
Captain O. M. Carter of the Engineer
Corps of the army, convicted by court
martial of irregularities in his accounts,
probably will be finally decided within a
few weeks. The papers are in the hands
of Attorney General Griggs, who has been
reviewing the case by direction of the
President. The Attorney General has
given much time to the findings of the
court and the evidence submitted. He
has personally examined every phase of
the case. To give him complete data all
the acounts of Captain Carter were sent
to the Department of Justice.
Briefs have been filed both for and
against Captain Carter, but to give him
self a complete understanding of the case
Attorney General Griggs has had an ex
pert bank examiner go over all of Captain
Carter's accounts and make a report. He
has consumed much time, as the papers
till two boxes about five feet high and
four feet square. Attorney General Griggs
has nearly completed his review, however,
and is now engaged in summing up state
ments of counsel and the reply of Colonel
Barr.
The World's Washington special says:
It is said to-night that President McKin
ley has at last shown a disposition to take
action in the case of Captain Carter, con
victed by court-martial of embezzling
$1,R00.000 of Government funds while su
perintending harbor work in Georgia.
President McKinley instructed Attorney
General Griggs to hasten his review of the
case and report not later than the last of
this week, that the President may an
nounce by that time his final determina
tion. The Attorney General to-day had
a Government bank official from New
York here giving a comparison of Captain
Carter's private bank account with his
official account.
Governor Roosevelt's recent visit to the
White House is considered by some to be
the chief cause of the President's deci
sion to take up the case in earnest. It is
said that th*Â» Governor told the President
frankly that his dilatoriness was bitterly
resented by the people

LOS ANGELES, July 10.â second I
annual convention of the Indian j
Teachers' Institute, that to-day met
in session in this city, marks a new
epoch in the Indian service and from it
will date a radical departure from former
custom, In the holding of yearly gather-
Ings of those who art: consecrating their
lives and ambitions to the upbuilding of
the Indian race.
These Indian Teachers' Institutes are
the outgrowth of the energy and execu
tive force of Miss Estelle Reel, the hrst
woman to hold the office of National Su
perintendent of Indian Schools. In dis
charging the duties of her office Dy in
specting the Indian schools in Kansas,
Oklahoma, Arizona, New Mexico. Wash
ington. Oregon and California during l v ;Â»7.
Miss Reel became familiar with the needs
of the Indian and as a sequence endeav
ored to bring about a broadening of the
minds that directed the educational
branch of the Indian service. Having this
in mind. Miss Heel recommended that an
institute for Indian teachers be held
where, emulating the practice of the
teachers of colleges and normal and pub
lic schools, these Government teachers
could meet, become personally acquaint
ed, exchange ideas and offer suggestions
that would prove beneficial in their voca
tion. The convention last year was held
in Colorado Springs, Col., and demon
strated the wisdom of establishing thes^
institutes.
To further aid the teachers in the In
dian service Miss Reel suggested to the
department that the institute for ISÂ»'J bd
held in Los Angeles at the time of the
National Educational Association conven
tion. The central idea that governed this
impulse was embodied in Miss Reel a
language: "We believe that every teacher
can learn, and 1 think that when the
teachers who come from every section of j
the United States meet In session it will
be possible for the teachers employed In
the Indian service to be able to study
method, by hearing eminent scholars dis
cuss pedagogic questions. The Influence
is for the better, and our teachers will be
made stronger and become Imbued with
a higher conception of their calling.
Hence the Indian Teachers' Institute will
this year become, during the daily ser
vices Of the National Educational Asso
ciation, a part of that natioual body for
instruction, advancement and improve
ment."
The second annual session of the In
dian Teachers' Institute was this morn-
Ing called to order at 10 o'clock by Miss
Reel, who introduced the Hon. Thomas J.
Kirk, State Superintendent of Public In
struction. Professor Kirk delivered an
address of welcome on behalf of the
State. Hon. Fred Eaton, Mayor of Los
Angeles, welcomed the teachers on behalf
of the municipality, while Dr. George W.
White, president of the University
of Southern California; Dr. E. T.
Pierce, principal of the Normal School,
and Professor J. Forhoy, Superintend
ent of the public schools of Los An
geles, welcomed the delegates on behalf
of the school Interests of the city, county
and State, the welcoming addresses con
cluding with, an address by Dr. Irvin
Shepard, general secretary of the Na

comed them on behalf of the teachers of
the nation, and bade them a hearty wel
come to the National Educational Asso

MISS ESTELLE REEL.
Woman Educator Who May Become
Wyoming's Governor.
Miss Etetelle Reel of Wyoming, who wa3
appointed Supe rintendent of Indian Schools
by President McKlnley, has a personality
and a career well calculated to give joy
to the man- or woman who is hopeful for
the future of the gentler sex. Miss Reel
is the kind of woman voter and woman
reformer to which the equal suffragist of
either sex delights to point. Pretty, gen
tle, educated, broad-minded, progressive,
she is possessed of a healthy egotism that
stands up for its own rights, while it is
never forgotful of the rights of others.
The new Indian School Superintendent is
first of all amply able to fill this Impor
tant government post.
It must be remembered that woman suf
frage is no bizarre idea in the upland
country of Wyoming, where the breezes
blow fresh and the men are more honest
than they are in the Eastern cities and at
low elevations. In Wyoming a woman is
regarded as capable of being as sensible,
as wise :m<l as learned as a man. The
idea of woman's equality may be said to
be organic there. That is why Miss Reel
is si widely admired and why she has so
much Influence.
That fact, too, explains why the people
Of Wyoming have seriously considered
Miss Keel for their Governor. Two years
ago she had a grand boom for the nomin
ation for Governor of Wyoming. That is
t" say, her friends then laid plans for her
nomination during the approaching fall.
Ir 1896 Miss Reel was ineligible for the
position of Governor of Wyoming. The
Governor must be at leasi 30, and at that
tim.- Miss Heel was only L'P. Sh>' has now
the age qualification and her friend.s be
lieve she has all the other qualifications
as well. But Miss Reel herself takes a
sensible view of the mutter. She disclaims
the ability to manage the affairs of a
State as well as the best man that Wyom
ing can find. She says that she does not
desire to be Governor. According to her.
the women of Wyoming (who poll half the
vote of the State, by the way) will be
satisfied with one of the six State offices
fi Â«r some time to come. This Is the office
of State Superintendent of Public In
struction.
Miss Reel prepared herself for the pro
f. ~sicn of an educator in the schools of
Chicago, St. Louis and Boston. She did
no little work in that line in Chicago be
fore coming West to grow up with the
country. She began her career in Chey
enne Â»k a teacher In one of the public
schools there, and her talent brought her
rapidly to the top. She was promoted
first to be the Superintendent of Instru â¢
tion for Laraxnie County â the most im
portant under-position of its kind in the
State. For foui years she filled that posi
tion admirably. Then she was made Stata
Superintendent, and now she is given the
highly Important post of Superintendent
of Indian Schools.
In her capacity as Superintendent of fr>
stri:eti< n fÂ«>r Wyoming Miss Reel has hell
the r.x tiflÃciO position of secretary of tne
Ht.tti> Board Of Charities. The duties of
th ; s board ait to conserve the State chir
[tnble and penal institutions. She has also
served ex-ofÃcic as secretary of the State
Land Board, which has charge of the
grants of land made to the State by Con
gress. Miss Reel has been very active :n
the matter of this land, and has fathere'l
several plans for its utilization. As sem
inary and register of the board she har
distributed several hundreds of thousinds

; Close Guard Kept Upon the Jurors

Special Dispatch to The Call.
WALLACE, Idaho, July 10.â Judge Stew- j
I art this morning- rendered his opinion In ;
' the actions brought by the Attorney- Gen- ;
â  eral for the removal of the County Com- ;
: nilssioners and Sheriff. He held that the i
charges had been proved to his sausfac
tion, and ordered their removal from of
fice. There were a number of charges
against the Commissioners, the chief
anmng: which was that of approving the
official bond of the Sheriff and that of the
Tax Collector in a sum less than that pro
vided by law. A few of the sureties on '
these bonds did not appear upon the as
sessment roll, and some had allowed their '
; taxes to become delinquent. In each case ;
legally qualified sureties were upon the
loads for more than the amount required
by law, but the court held that the suro
tlea must qualify In double the amount.
required, and that therefore the Commis-
B had not performed their duty in
accepting the bonds. There were several
other charges but they were of a trivial .
nature.
Judge Stewart rendered a lengthy opin
lon, i ov< ring eighteen pages of type-writ
ten matter. In the case against the Sheriff
. the charge was failure to prevent the. riot
' uf April 29 and refusing to Identify par
ticipants therein. The Sheriff has main
tained that he was unable to recognise
any one in the mob, but the court thought
otherwise and ordered his removal from
otnee for failure to perform his duty.
The Commissioners have been allowed
the, llbertj of the town for several days,
but the Sheriff has been confined in the
Wallace jail, and is still kept there. An
appeal will be taken to the Supreme- Court
of the State.
Governor Steunenberg has been here for
several days and will at once appoint
Commissioners to till the places declared
vacant by the court. The Commissioners,
under the Idaho law, will appoint a Sher- :
Iff.
The trial of Paul Corcoran began in
earnest this afttrnoon. Acting Prosecut
ing Attorney Forney made his opening
statement to the jury before luncl
outlined the ca- State. He stated â¢
| that the prosecution would prove a con- |

e of this conspiracy puna and masks
were placed In the vi lon halls In Burke,
Gem and Mullan; that on the morning
of the "lUh the union miners at Burke
repaired to their hall, where they armed
and masked and marched to the depot,
where they captured the train which took
them to Wardner; that they were met
at Gem and Wallace by delegations from
i the other unions and at Wardner by
. members of that union. Forney premised â 
' to show that the defendant was a raov
ing spirit in this conspiracy, and that he
! was one of the men who shot at Chi
for whose murder he 1b now being tried.
In the afternoon but two witnesses
wore examined, one of whom. Sheriff
, Young, refused a number of times to i
: testify on the ground that he was In the
custody of the military authorities and
I did no"t know what charge might be
1 placed against him, and that his answer
might incriminate him. He only answ< .-â  d
questions on being ordered so to do by

The other witness. Thomas Ames,
prover rather favorable to the defense,
stifled that he was a member of the
â  Wardner Miners' Union, and that h<

TO TEACH AT STANFORD.
Introduction of Two New Courses of
Study.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, July 10.â
'Die following two announcements in re
gard to courses that will be given at
Stanford University next year were made
from the office of the president to-day:
Rev. Charles R. Brown of the First Con
gregational Church of Oakland will give
a course of lectures on "The Ethics of
Christ" at 1:30 p. m. each Monday after
noon during the second semester of the
year Dr. Brown was recently offered
the chair of personal ethics at Stanford
by President David Starr Jordan, but his
Oakland congregation finally persuaded
him to remain with it. This course on
"The Ethics of Christ" will afford a fine
opportunity, however, for the Stanford
students to become acquainted with Dr.
Brown.
Professor Henry Harrison Haynes. in
structor In Semitic languages in the
Divinity School at San Mateo, will give a
course "in Hebrew at the university three
days a week throughout the year. Mr.
Haynea has taken the degrees of master
of arts and bachelor of sacred theology
and was this year made a doctor of
philosophy by Harvard University. His
principal study at Harvard was Semitic
philology and his doctor's dissertation
was entitled "The Sabaean Inscription
Glaser 554."
INJECTED INTO THE BRAIN.
Anti-Toxin Serum Used to Save a
Patient's Life.
NEW YORK. July 10.â Anti-toxin serum
was injected into a man's brain at Pas
sai . N. J.. at the general hospital late
this afternoon to save his life. The pa
tient, who was attacked by tetanus, Is
Joseph A. Grotsky, a lawyer and Justice
of the Peace of I'a.ssaic. He was injured
on July 4 while shooting off blank cart
ridges. One of the charges entered the
palm of his left hand, inflicting severe
wounds. Blood-poisoning developed on
Saturday and lockjaw set in early on
Monday morning. The doctors hope to
sav<> his life. The first patient to be in
oculated with scrum in this country was
operated on in the general hospital at
Passaic a year ago and recovered. He
was A. M. Hemion.

MENTIONS LONG SEA SERVICE
IN HIS REPORT.
The United States Local Inspectors
at New York Refuse to Accept
the Excuse and Suspend
Him for Two Years.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, July 10.â The report of
Captain Watkins of the stranding of the
American steamship Paris was made pub
lic to-day by the local Board of Steam
boat Inspectors. Captain Watkins makes
no attempt to evade responsibility for th.c
accident, but on the contrary says frank
ly that the stranding of the. ship. was the
result of an unaccountable error on his
part. The accident, he says, was not
due to any want of thought or anxiety
about his vessel, but resulted from a mis
take he made in calculating the position
of the ship.
The local inspectors have suspended
Captain Watkins' license as master of
ocean steamers for two years.
Captain Watkins Is still in charge of the
Paris and is acting in the interests of
the underwriters, to whom the ship was
turned over by the American line offi
cials.
Captain Watkins' report was made un
der oath. After reciting the incidents at
tending the striking of the vessel, the
rescue of the passengers and crew, the
saving of the valuable cargo and the sub
sequent efforts to tloat the steamship, the
captain says:
"I regret to say that the casualty was
owing to an unaccountable error on my
It is about 131 miles from Cape La
Hogue to the Lizard, and the run be
tween these points I calculated occupied
urs and forty-five minutes. Unfort
unately, I reckoned the time on this basis,
for coming up with the Lizard from 7:35 '
a. in., when abeam of the Casquets. in
stead of from 7::: S. when abeam of Cape
La I. gue, and the vessel was thus, really
eighteen miles ahead of the position I

departure from Cape Lra Hogue at 7:38,
when he was actually abreast of the Cas
nuets at that time, a distance of about
sixteen and one-half miles. This of course
would put him sixteen and one-half miles
ahead of his reckoning, but would not ac
count for his In ing set into the northward
of his course seven miles, allowing his in
tention to pass the Lizard at a distance of
three miles. As Captain "\Vatkins does
not make any mention of making allow
ance for tide, we are of the opinion he had
not taken that matter into consideration,
whereas, when he approached the English
coast he must have encountered the flood
tide on his port bow, setting him toward
the land. In addition to this he makes no
mention of having used his lead and he
only discovered it was thick over the land
when the ship was ashore.
"Giving all due consideration to these
matters, we are of the opinion that Cap
tain "Watkins has not acted with the ne
cessary care and attention requisite in the
navigation of his vessel which a master
should have used and we therefore sus
pend his license as master for ocean
steamers for a period of two years."

CONSIDERATION
POSTPONED
Attorney Archibald Barnard of the
Tfr-pubHcan County Central Committee
appeared last evening before the Elec
tion Commission and, speaking for the
committee, nested that considera
tion of its petition for a blanket
ticket be deferred until such ti ...ft as the
entire commission could be present. T.
J. Peridergast, also of the committee.
agreed in this with Mr. Barnard, and
after some little discussion the com
mission consented to postpone it to 11
o'clock to-morrow morning.
In making his lues.t Mr. Barnard
said: "It is the desire of the Central
Committee, for which 1 speak, that
consideration of its petition be deferred
until all of the members of the com
mission are present, but at the same
time we do not want to wait longer
than Wednesday, and i would ask that
that day be set."
Commissioners Lane, Wells and
Sheehan, who were the only members
on hand, consulted a moment, and Lane
announced that Mayor Phelan would
not return from his vacation until Sat
urday, but that- he would set Wednes
day as the day.
"I want to ask Mr. Lane if he is still
of the opinion that our plan is not fea
sible; he so expressed himself to me
the other day," broke in Pendergast.
"I am certainly of the same opinion,"
retorted Lane. "I don't think your
scheme is feasible, and don't think it
will go.".
The commission then took up the se
lection of election officers, and a discus
sion, which gave every promise of be-
Ing heated, was staved off un
til to-morrow.

JEFFRIES WELCOMED HOME.
ENGLAND'S IDEA OF SHAMROCK.
LOS ANGELES
WELCOMES THE
NEW CHAMPION
Jeffries in His Old
Home.
â¢
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS AXGELES. July 10.â It has been
a long time since Los Angeles sports
have had an opportunity to welcome a
champion, and to say that they did not
do it to the highest degree of perfection
to-day would be a misstatement of
facts. James J. Jeffrie?, the champion
heavy-weight of the world, is again in
the city that for eighteen years he has
called "home." The champion arrived
at 2 o'clock, forty minutes later than
was expected. Thousands assisted in
the welcome, and the big, bashful
champion bowed right and left as he
was whirled through the crowded thor
oughfares from the Arcade depot to the
Hollenbeck Hotel.
As the champion stepped from the
rear of the train President John Brink
of the Los Angeles Athletic Club and
Tom Darmody entwined his neck with
a huge wreath of flowers, composed of
the national colors. The blue flowers
were "bachelor buttons," and the red
and white flowers were roses and car
nations. The train that bore the young
boilermaker was filled with teachers,
and the presence of thousand:- uf per
sons that surged around the depot
caused a murmur of comment, but
when the instructors heard the cheers
for Jeffries a college yell and a Vassar
<-ull were mingled with the huzza of the
sports of the older school. Jeffries was
received as a hero. He was escorted up
town in a buggy that was preceded by
the Seventh Regiment band and fol
lowed by a dozen tally-hos. As soon as
he could escape from the crowd, short
ly after 3 o'clock, "Jim," with only one
thought in his. mind, hurried to his
East Los Angeles home to greet the
woman that is more to him than any
thing else in this world â his mother.
"Brother Jack" accompanied "Jim,"
and the meeting between the sons and
the mother was sweetly pathetic.
To-night Jeffries was serenaded and
later driven to the Burbank Theater,
where the champion boxed four rounds,
ar.d then with his brother, '"Jack" Jef
fries," gave an illustration of the tenth
and eleventh rounds of his battle with
Robert Fitzsimmons, including the
knockout blow.
Three ten-round m goes at cntrn
weights were a part' of the evening's
programme They were between Ben
Trimble and Tim Ryan, Mike Thornton
and "Gypsy Kid." and Young Fitzsim
mons and Clarence McKee. Jeffries
rf .f,, rf . r .ri the contests. The crowd filled
the house from pit to flnmc
Jeffries says he is anxious to meet
Sharkey.
"I will fight him before th*> club of
fering the biggest purse, but I much
D.r< f< r to fight on the roast. I am here
for a rest, and I shall take it with my
people in the little home on the Arroyo
Seco."

THE SA!N FKAJN CISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JULY 11, 1899.
SPORT
CREW WILL BE
PENSIONED IF
SHAMROCK WINS
One Pound a Week
for Life.
Special Cable to Th? Call and tn* N>w York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1S0&, by James Gor- j
d'.n Bennett.
SOUTHAMPTON. July 10.â Rumor
has it that Sir Thomas Lipton has
promised each of the crew one pound a
week for life if the Shamrock wins.
The sailmakers have to-day been busy ;
on board the Shamrock making many
j trifling alterations to the canvas which
Saturday's spin showed to be neces
i sary.
People are talking and writing a
great deal about the coming trial of
the Shamrock with the Britannia. Now.
let me tell you on the best authority
that Mr. Fife is' not going to allow
these trials to give any indication of
what the Shamrock is capable of doing.
People will know as little after these
i p. '-called trials as before. Independently
j of the care that Mr. Fife will take to
i ward this end, there Is the facfthat the
i Shamrock, as she Is now, is a different
boat from what she will be when she
sails at New York. Now her inside is
stiff' nt-d with steel girders. These will
not be removed until she gets ready
for her races in New York. This change
i will entirely alter her conditions at sta
bility.
The cleverest yachtsmen here â those
closely connected with the Shamrock â
express themselves with the utmost
skepticism regarding the trials between
the Columbia and Defender, holding
that such trials cannot possibly have
been held with the object of making
any real race between the boats. If it
were so, they maintain, it is lack of pol
icy, but they conclude that the trials
have been for negative purposes, such
as those that will be made here in a
few days; in fact, rather as "a blind,"
Anyhow, the next time the Shamrock
&,<â :<' out we shall at least have the
pleasure of making the acquaintance of
her jack-yard, topsail, flying jib, bal
loon foresail and spinnaker, which up
to the present have remained on the
hulk of a schooner lying alongside of
the Sh mrock, upon which her crew
lives. With these set we shall be able
to judge a little better whether she is
as stiff as she appeared on Saturday.
The schooner opon which the crew
lives lies on the port side "of the Sham- -
rock. No one sleeps on board the
yacht, although there is a constant
watch throughout the night. There is
no accommodation aboard the racer.
Here, as well as in New York, the
hands will live away from the cutter.
Here they are dwelling very comfort
ably on ;ir, <>M hulk. In New York they
will be on a sort of house boat, where
they will live without shore liberty un
til the races are over. This all the men
understood, and although they have
not signed their indentures yet, they will
do so, including the conditions named.
most willingly, such is their enthu
siasm.
A nattier crow it would be difficult to
find. All are bright, alert young fel
low?, with mastheadsmen among thorn,
nimble as cats, picked so that they
stay at the masthead throughout an
entire race.

MOTOR CYCLE CRAZE GROWING.
BASEBALL IN THE EAST.
MOTOR-PACE
FEVER ATTACKS
BICYCLE MEN
Many Want to Follow
Machines.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, July 10. â Cycle racing
men have been attacked by the motor !
pace fever. Just at present its
ravages are confined to. professional
riders, but later it promises to grip <.y
--cle race patrons in even more virulent j
form. Its sudden outbreak is coincident '
with the sudden coining of Miller and;
Linton with their Parisian naphtha
tandems, and of August Cabaillat, the
most expert steersman of motor cycles
in France.
With a suddenness that proves the
force of the fever a score of American ,
professional riders, who have made rep- ;
Utations in other distances, have an- i
nounced their candidacy for the mid- â¢
die-distance paced honors. As many
more riders of scarcely less repute have
as promptly offered their services on !
the motor machines that have already
been negotiated for the promoters
caught with the infection have hurri
edly altered their programmes of the
immediate future,- and one concern, the
Atlantic Athletic Association, an
nounced to-day that a big motor record
race will contribute to the feature at
each of its meets at Manhattan Beach
during the remainder of the season.
A factor in the races will be Linton
and his crack French tandems. His ma
chines differ from those of American
make, and Linton claims that they are
faster. His crew has had experience
in navigating motor cycles, whereas the
American crews will enter the lists
comparatively raw. Cabaillat, the
steersman, is referred to as "The Devil
<>n Wheels" on the tracks of his own ;
country. He will take his machine ]
through to port in a crowd that scarce- j
ly another rider would attempt. He
holds a certificate from the French
authorities attesting his capability t.>
drive racing cars of fifteen-horse power
over the roads of that republic.
With Cabaillat rides Dudley Marks,
whose knowledge of mechanics makes
him a safe engineer. Marks, occupying
a rear seat, is able to coach Linton be
hind him. Cabaillat and Marks assert
that they can drive tandems at the
rate of thirty-six miles an hour. If
Linton is able to follow them at that
,rate he will beat the world's record for
distance, held by Elkes, by nearly two
miles.
Ltnton's tandems differ slightly from
the motor machine brought over from
France by Henri Fournier. The cogs
in that of the latter are replaced by
chains in Linton's machines. Like
Fournier's tandem, Linton's motor cy
cles have two horsepower capacity.
Miller's tandem is exactly the same
as those owned by the Welshman. Both
Miller and Linton were greatly exer- j
cised because they were forced to pay i
a duty of 45 per cent on their motors
when they landed In New York. They
had expected to bring them in on the
same ground as theatrical effects.

NEW YORK, July 10.â Matty Matthews
i of Brooklyn defeated Otto Zc-iloff of Chi
cago in the ninth round of a vicious right
at the Coney Island Sporting Club to
i night. Matthews was by far the cleverer
1 and used both hands relentlessly on his
j opponent's anatomy. In the ninth Zeiloff
: staggered helplessly and Matthews
dropped his hands as he said to George
i Edwards, the referee:
"I don't want to hit him any more."
Edwards took the hint and stopped the
bout right there by orderfng the men to
i their corners and declaring Matthews the
â  winner.
Frank Patterson of Brooklyn and Solly
: Smith of Los Angeles fought a twenty
five round draw at 122 pounds. The men
fought savagely, but were strong at the
finish. Patterson was frequently cau
tioned for hitting low and was disquali
! fled in the twenty-third round for repeat-
Ing the offense. Smith begged the referee
to lot the bout go on and so lost, a favor
-1 able doclelon. The attendance was 6000.
MYSTERY OF A
PHYSICIAN'S
SUDDEN DEATH
Dr. Schlarbaum Dies
in a Lodging-House.
USED A FICTITIOUS NAME
DEADLY POISONS FOUND IN
THE ROOM HE OCCUPIED.
Deceased Had Been Suffering From
Asthma for Many Years, Which
May Have Ended His
Life.
Dr. John Christian Benedictine Schlar
baum, a physician prominent in lodge
| circles of this city and Oakland, was
'â  found dead in a lodging-house at 119 Third
street yesterday afternoon. There Is somo
doubt as to the cause of his death, ami
the Coroner has ordered an investigation.
The presence of deadly poisons In the
room would Indicate suicide, but the. fact
I that the man had been suffering from
asthma for many years strengthens the ,
belief that he succumbed to the disease.
On Sunday evening Dr. Schlarbaum
registered under a fictitious name at the
hotel and apked for a room in the upper
portion of the building.- He said that he
had been suffering from asthma and was
in a very bad condition. He went to bed.
but did not appear next day. and at 3
o'clock his room was entered by the clerk,
who found him sitting in a chair, dear!.
i From all appearances he had been vomit-
I Ing and had been In a distressed condition.
When the t'oroner arrived he found a
bottle of cyanide of potassium and one of
muriatic acid concealed in a bureau
drawer in the room. They were about the
only property belonging to the deceased
â  which could ho found. Investigation
i proved that instead of being J. B. (illes.
| as whs on the register, the body was that
of Dr. Schlarbaum. This was ascertained
J. C. B. SCHLARBAUM.
from the name on a number of lodge
receipts found in his pockets and from
the initials on his shirt front.
Among the other papers were several
prescriptions for morphine tablets filled
at a local pharmacy. Inquiry at the drug
store corroborated the statement that
the doctor had been suffering some time
from asthma.
Dr. Schlarbaum graduated from the Cal
ifornia Medical College in this city on
December 12, 1888. He practiced medicine
for some years and had an office m the
Nevada block. Recently he abandoned
his profession on account of his health and
took up mining. A few days ago he re
turned from San Jose, having been to the
Interior portions of the State on prospect
ing trips. The presence of the poisons in
the room can be accounted for by the fact
that both are used in certain milling pro
cesses.
The doctor became a member of Live
Oak Lodge No. 61, F. and A. M., in ISS7.
â He was also a member of Rio Grande
Chapter No. 4, Royal Arch Masons, of
Albuquerque. N. M., and Pilgrim Com
mandery, Knights Templar, of the same
place. He was a member of the Independ
ent Order of Odd Fellows and also of the
National Eclectic Medical Association of
Forest. Ohio. He was about 55 years of.
age and leaves no known relatives.

BANTAM-WEIGHTS MATCHED.
Palmer and McGovern Will Battle for
the Championship.
NEW YORK, July 10.â Pedlar Palmer,
the bantam-weight champion of England,
and Terry McGovern, believed to be tha
best bantam in this country, have been
matched for the championship of the
world. Dr. A. P. Ordway, representing
Palmer, and Sam E. Harris, the manager
of McGovern, met in this city to-day and
signed articles calling for a twenty-five
round brut at 116 pounds, for the cham
pionship of the world, the men to meet
before the club offering the largest purse.
The fight is to be decided the latter part
of August. The winner will take 75 per
cent and the loser 25 per cent of the
purse.
The directors of the Westchester Club
agreed to give 50 per cent of the gross re
ceipts, with a guarantee of 110.000, a per
centage of the receipts in excess of $20,
--000, and to each boxer 15 per cent of the
receipts from any pictures that may be
taken.
Plaviner for Whist Trophies.
CHICAGO, July 10.â The tournament of
the American Whist League opened to
day at the Auditorium Hotel. Many of
the foremost whist players in the United
States were present. The first play was
for the Brooklyn trophy. The contestants
represented the New England Whist As
sociation, the New York State Whist As
sociation, the Atlantic Whist Association,
the Chicago Whist Club and the North
western Whist Association.
Falls Down a Mine Shaft.
JAMESTOWN. July 10.â William Snail
fell from the skip at the Jumper mine
this morning and was instantly killed.
The skip ascended to the 200-foot level,
where Snail, with several others, left it.
Instead of getting off on the ripht side.
Snail stepped off to the left on a beam
of the compartment shaft and fell to the
400-foot level. He leaves a widow and
children at Jamul. San Diego County. He
was about 53 years old.

THE BUBONIC BCfIRD OF HEALTH-
WITH the abortive attempt to create a scare by
a report of the appearance of the bubonic j
igue in this city, the Board of Health ap
reward the followers and to serve the pur- ;
- of Dan Burns and the Southern Pacific Com- ,
n it? career and gave the public an illustra
:' the tactics on which it relies for success.
The board cannot be accused of a lack of energy nor
of a lack of audacity. Scarcely was it in office when it
:. looking around for a chance to serve its mas
ters and to gain prestige for itself. It did not wait
lÂ«.r an opportunity to come. It made one out of what
was virtually nothing. It did not restrain itself in
moderation in exploiting of the manufactured op
portunity, but set about starting a panic with the au
dacity of men who have no scruples and are reckless
hat may be the consequences to others so long
mething be gained for themselves.
. Dr. Barbat, who serves Dan Burns as family phy
sician and as representative on the Board of Health,
a brother who has a laboratory and who is ser
viceable to the board as a '"bacteriologist." The will
ing germ-hunter examined the bodies of two Japan
ese aho were drowned in trying to swim ashore from
the Nippon Maru and declared he found evidence that
they were infected with the bubonic plague â the most
dreadful pestilence of the pestilential Orient. Thus it
was made to appear that the plague had reached San
Francisco, and the board got ready to profit by the
which was expected to follow.
Two advantages were to be gained from the panic.
In tl ice a great and valuable service was to
xhv I'acihc Mail Steamship Company
iuring a rival line. If the public could be per
ed that the competing line of steamships brought,
[ence to the city, Huntington would have on the
n something like the monopoly he abuses on
That far the service was rendered to the rail
road bosses, and no doubt the bosses appreciated it at
its true worth.
The second service was to Dan Burns himself. Had
the board and its bacteriologist succeeded in causing
a general alarm, it could with more pretension ask |
from the Supervisors a large appropriation, ostensibly:
for the purification and the protection of the health of'
the city, but really to make places for the idle and;
worthless members of the Burns push. Thus the .
board would have increased its prestige in the city
and augmented the strength of the Burns ring.
The attempt to create a scare was abortive, but it
none the less succeeded in doing the city considerable
harm. In the East the character of the Board of
Health is not understood, nor are its purposes com
prehended. Therefore the story of the bubonic plague ',
in this city has been widely published there and ex
aggerated. Many a tourist who might have come to
San Francisco has been turned away by this false
rumor started for the purpose of making business for
the Pacific Mail and doing politics for Dan Burns.
Thus the first effect of the operations of the board has
been to injure the whole community to an extent
which it is not possible at this time to estimate.
From the way the board has begun it is easy to
foresee how it will continue. The welfare of the com
munity is to count as nothing. The main object is to
devise a means of forcing the Supervisors to increase
the appropriations for the board. Fortunately, the
first move was so gross a blunder that the people can
readily perceive the nature of the trap that was pre
pared. Hereafter the public will be wary. It will take
something more than a report from the bacteriologist
Barbat to start a scare or induce the Supervisors to
intrust more public money for the pay of the Burns
push.
Oom Paul is said to be fencing diplomatically with
British statesmen to gain time until the rainy season
sets in and military operations in the Transvaal will be
doubly dangerous for British troops. Oom Paul must
be reading the reports of General Otis from the Phil
ippines.
Mrs. Yda Addis Storke of Santa Barbara must
think that Attorney Jackson has four or five lives.
Prussic acid, chloroform and two pistols certainly
ought to silence an ordinary lawyer forever.
Senator Gorman's friends claim that his health is
poor. It is not half so poor as his chances for the
Presidential nomination.
MR. BARRETT, DIPLOMAT.
DURING Mr. Cleveland's last term everybody
was surprised by his discovery of Mr. John
Barrett, and his appointment of that person
as Minister to Siam. Mr. Barrett's experience in
diplomacy consisted of inexperience in journalism,
which honorable profession he had failed to adorn as
a reporter for a paper in Oregon. He moved on the
land of the white elephant, and longed for great
things. Our British brethren have been for years
carefully cultivating the spirit of imperialism in all
American Consuls and Ministers in Asia. Colonel
Denby was carefully coached in that line by the Brit
ish Minister in Peking. He had not been there long
until he sent up a loud and bitter complaint about the
quarters furnished him to live in. The British Min
ister had a compound all to himself, and servants in
livery and raiment that tied honors with the yellow
jacket and peacock feather of old Li Hung Chang.
Colonel Denby stayed in Peking twelve years and saw
the British Minister lord it over the Celestials until j
he longed to represent an empire and take np the
white man's burden of kicking some poor devil
smaller than himself. The same influences were '<
around Diplomat Barrett at Bangkok. The English
newspaper there received him with a flourish of head- j
lines and immediately acquired an American circula- j
tion, for he sent marked copies to everybody in the
country of whom he had ever heard. The Bangkok
Times and Barrett were like organ and organist. He
settled ah American's claim against Chulalongkorn
for killing an elephant and burning a woodpile. The
Times hailed it as a diplomatic triumph that would
have roused the envy of Hastings and Clive and that
had made all Asia tremble with fear while not thrilled
with admiration. To Mr. Barrett the Philippine war
was an opportunity. Having been taught by his Brit
ish tutors in Siam that the people of Asia are not to
be treated as human beings, he was a tool filed and
fitted to the British need of some one to keep the
English imperial policy in countenance. He has re
turned to his own, his native land, like Denby, to mi
i struct the mere American taxpayer, who only fur
j nishes the money and merely sends his sons to be
j killed or rot alive under a vertical sun, in his duty to
! violate the constitution in order that Barretts and
I Denbys may have whole compounds and liveries and
!do diplomacy in Asia up to the British standard. He
j has been, and probably still is, raising his voice in the
j Eastern States in favor of imperialism and in convey
i ance of gross misinformation about the Philippines.
j His representation of the beauties of a tropical cli
i mate and its adaptation to our race is the result either
;of great ignorance or unbridled mendacity. Races
j are found planted by nature where the physical condi
tions are adapted to them. Our race was placed in
j the temperate zone, and the tropical races were
I planted in the torrid zone. When Mr. Barrett can
i point to any estimable number of temperate zone peo
! pie born in the third generation in the tropics, he may
'' relieve himself of a suspicion of being either a fool or
I a falsifier.
But it is when he discusses the politics of imperial
ism that Barrett shines with all the brilliancy of a '
mud nimbus. He declares that we must keep the
Philippines because we bought them from Spain, and
must hold them, no matter what may be the objection
of the people. In discussing alternatives he says:
"We cannot honorably sell the Philippines, for we will
be bartering away 8,000,000 natives, unless they them
selves favor said sale."'
That illuminates the Barrett intellect. We may make
war on Spain because she governed people against
their consent. We bought the Philippines without
consent of the natives, and are making devastating
war on them to establish our government over them
without their consent. Yet, cries Barrett, "it will be
dishonorable to sell them without their consent." Is
it more dishonorable to sell than to buy people with
out their consent? Mr. Barrett has at last furnished
an imperial standard of honor. It is honorable to buy
an unwilling people, to make destructive war upon
them and to answer their cry for mercy with the de
mand for unconditional surrender, to call them rebels
because they resist being sold to us, but it would be
dishonorable to sell them to another buyer unless they
are willing!
Long may Barrett's diplomatic tongue wag as an
imperialist tocsin!
THE MAYOR AND_THE: PLEDGE.
\ VIGOROUS and persistent efforts are now being
made to induce the Supervisors to increase the
appropriation set apart for the Fire Department
in the estimates for the coming year recently reported
by the Finance Committee. Protests against any re
duction in the effective force of the department come
from all quarters, and it is not to be denied that in
many respects the arguments of the protesters are
well founded. If, however, the estimate for the Fire
Department be increased there must be a correspond
ing deduction made from the appropriation of some
other branch of the municipal administration, or the
dollar limit pledge must be broken.
To obtain counsel and advice as to the best means
of providing adequately within the dollar limit for the
needs of all departments of the city government, the
Supervisors have referred the estimates to the Mer
chants' Association for consideration. The judgment
of that association will undoubtedly be valuable. The
members of it are men who have long taken an active
interest in municipal affairs and are strenuous sup
porters of progress with economy. They desire to see
an efficient administration of the city, a liberal policy
of improvements in streets, sewers, parks and other
public works, and they also desire the maintenance of
that dollar limit pledge to which most of them are
bound by their public declarations as clearly as are
the Supervisors by their campaign promises.
The Merchants' Association, however, is, after all,
an unofficial body. It has not been charged by the
responsibilities of office to make a thorough and com
prehensive study of the relation of municipal finances
to the work of administration. The counsel it will
give will doubtless be excellent, but it cannot be final.
The Supervisors, after receiving the report from the
association, should hold it for the consideration of the
Mayor. That officer, as chief executive of the city, is
charged with the duty of investigating all such mat
ters, and his advice ought by all means to be sought
and obtained by the Supervisors before they fix the
estimates and the appropriations for the year.
The expediency of consultation with the Mayor is
exceptionally strong in this case by reason of the fact
that Mr. Phelan is not only Mayor of the city, but is
and has been ever since he entered office the most
conspicuous advocate in public life of the dollar
limit. He has repeatedly asserted that a revenue de
rived from a tax rate of $i on the $100 on the basis of
an assessed valuation of taxable property of $350,000.
--ooa, would be amply sufficient for all the needs of the
municipality. He has insisted that it would provide
not only for the maintenance in unimpaired vigor of
all departments of the government, but for such im
provements as the progress of the city demands.
It was because of public confidence in these asser
tions made by Mr. Phelan after two years in office
that he was re-elected Mayor in an election in which
his party suffered overwhelming defeat. To him,
THE SAX FRAy CISCO CALL, TUESDAY, JULY 11, 1899.
therefore, does a considerable body of the people look
for counsel in the problem now confronting the mu
nicipality. The Supervisors should obtain his aid in
shaping the estimates. He should not be deprived of
any of the glory of a successful solving of the com
plication, nor should he be permitted to shirk any of
the responsibility should evil result from reductions in
the appropriations asked for by the heads of depart
ments.
As for the Supervisors themselves, their duty is
clear. They were elected upon a pledge to limit the
tax rate to one dollar on the hundred. Let them keep
that pledge in letter and in spirit, and the public will ,
have no right to condemn them. â¢
THE COMING YfJCHT RfICE.
REPORTS concerning the trials made by the Co
lumbia and the Shamrock give reason for be
lieving the coming yacht race will be the best
ever sailed. Both the challengers and the defenders
seem well satisfied with their boats, and apparently
' : have good reason for their satisfaction.
The Columbia not only made a successful run in her
trial trip, but since then has been matched with the
De;'tnder, that held the cup against the Valkyrie, and
| has shown herself to be a fleeter and a better yacht in
| everj respect than that famous and victorious craft.
The last contest between the two was in some re
bpects unfortunate, inasmuch as both yachts sailed the
wtcng course, but the error did not in any way pre
vent a fair test of the comparative merits of the two.
The race was sailed over a twenty-six mile course,
: and in covering that distance the Columbia outsailed
the Defender by three minutes and thirteen seconds.
Granting the Defender a time allowance of two mm:
-: utes, the Columbia will still have the victory by more
than a minute. From the results of the trial experts
draw the conclusion that since the Columbia wins
over a twenty-six mile course by that much time in
her present condition, she can, when properly tuned
iup by next September, beat 'the Defender over a
thirty mile course by as much as five minutes, which
is all that is required of her.
While the Columbia is thus satisfying to the expec
tations of Americans, the Shamrock has given hardly
less satisfaction to British yachtsmen. She has not
yet been tested in a race against a fleet yacht of the
first class, but her trial trip has been remarkably suc
cessful. One of the experts who watched her is re
ported to have said: '"We have a flyer. We have a
yacht much better than anything before known here.
Ii the Americans beat us they will have to work hard
for it. They are not going to have a run away
triumph."
From all reports it appears the Shamrock, much
more than the Columbia, is a novelty in the way of
yacht construction. In this contest the Americans
! are the conservatives and the Irish challenger is the
! radical innovator. It is said there is scarcely a bit of
wood about her. Her entire hull is of thin metal.
The mainmast is of hollow steel and all the rigging is
of wire. No expense has been spared in fitting her.
Her sails are not made of common canvas, but of cloth
woven from the finest sea island cotton, and represent
the best work of men who claim to be the foremost ;
sailma"kers in the world.
For all that she has been constructed in a British |
shipyard, the Shamrock will be essentially an Irish
yacht, as her owner designed her to be. Her crew
is Irish, and in the recent trial trip their work elicited
no little praise by reason of its swiftness, its efficiency
and the clock-like regularity with which every portion
of it was performed.
All that yacht builders can do to make the great '
race a satisfactory contest between the two foremost
racing and sponing people in the world has been done. ;
The rest remains for the yachtsmen and their crews.
The irritation caused by the Dunraven outbreak over
the last race has passed away from both sides, and the j
public can look forward to a contest of friendly rivalry
in skill and seamanship.
DOMESTIC SERVICE IN BOSTON.
â¢ "ROM a time so remote that the memory of man
runneth not to the contrary there has been a
sound of lamentation in the United States over
the problems of domestic service, and the burden of
the wailing has been that American girls, through a
false pride, object to such service and refuse to per- 1
form it. Now, however, there comes from the East
a story of a nature so contrary to the old one it has
all the interest of a genuine surprise party.
In a recent number of the Independent Mrs. Eliza
beth Stuart Phelps Ward, who lives in the vicinity
of Boston, asserts that in "the present chaotic condi
tion of American society many a woman in domestic
service may be as well born and as well educated as
her employer. Hundreds of women are ready to en
ter kitchens and serve in dining-rooms who would not
have considered such a step possible fifteen years
ago."
In proof of her assertion Mrs. Ward cites her own
experience and says: "Some time since I advertised
for a woman to do the housework of my family and
received eighty applications, of which not more than
two or three were from professional servants. Dress- j
makers, clerks, saleswomen, widows, nurses, stenog- j
raphers, respectable American women by the score, :
were ready to ask admission to a home where they
would be well treated, and perform its roughest du
ties. The widow of a physician, a well-bred lady, her
self accustomed in her past life to her own servants, j
applied to a suburban household for permission to j
carry on its domestic work. The same day brought a !
similar request from a teacher educated in three of our
leading institutions â one a college."
This story is proof of much more than of the point
it was cited to prove. It is an evidence not only of
the breaking down of former prejudice against domes
tic service, but also of the increasing severity of the*
struggle for existence in the older States. When an i
educated teacher, a graduate of three of the leading !
institutions of New England, and manifestly indus- ;
trious and willing to work, has to seek and almost
beg for employment as a servant, it is evident condi
tions have changed since the days when even the
poorest American girl scorned to be a servant and
would hardly consent to be a "help."
Mrs. Ward's statement is a surprise indeed, and the
surprise is not a pleasant one. It changes the aspect
of the servant girl problem, however, in an important
respect. Since college-bred girls have taken to
service for a living, it is clearly the duty of the em
ploying class to meet them half way and make the
living worth having.
John J. Valentine, the Wells-Fargo president, may
be sorry some day that he did not give a pass to Rev.
V. Marshall Law. He may have to sue some other
spiritual director some day for a pass on his last
journey.
Devils Isle seems to have been appropriately
named. His Satanic Majesty appears to have sta
tioned a few of his deputies there to make life inter
esting for Dreyfus.
Bill Bryan is casting about for a running mate. If
i hii expansion ideas undergo no change, he may yet
, select Aguinaldo.

GENERAL SCHWAN ARRIVES EN

Captain Edward H. Plummer, senior aid
1 to Major General Shaft er, was agreeably
surprised yesterday to receive a telegTam
from Assistant Adjutant General Carter
| informing him that he had been commis
sioned lieutenant colonel of the Thirty
fifth Infantry Volunteers. Captain Plum-
I mer's promotion la well deserved and
comes without solicitation on his part, !
although it is said the appointment was I
recommended by General Shatter. As
: Colonel Kobbe of the Third Artillery, who
, has been â  appointed to command the i
Thirty-fifth, is now at Manila, the or- j
ganization of the regiment will probably i
be left entirely with the lieutenant colonel.
The regiment will he organized at Van
couver barracks and will be recruited en
! tirely from the Pacific Coast.
Captain Plummer is a native of Mary
land and is regarded as one of the most
efficient officers of the regular army. He i
entered "West Point July 1, IST3, as a cadet \
J from Maryland. On his graduation on j
June 15, IST", he was appointed a second
lieutenant in the Tenth United States j
Infantry. On November 29, 1894, he was j
promoted to the first lieutenancy in the !
same regiment. He was commissioned a
captain in the Tenth on April 26, 1888. At
the outbreak of the war with Spain he
was assigned to the Fifth Army Corps
under General Shafter. During the cam
paign around Santiago he was quarter
master at General Shafter's headquarters.
The proficiency with which he performed j
his duty as quartemaster attracted the !
most favorable comment from his superior i
officers and ultimately obtained for him
the position of senior aid upon the staff
of General Shafter. When General Shafter j
returned from Cuba Captain Plummer ac- i
companied him, and since that time he
has been the general's principal assistant. !
Lieutenant Colonel M. P. Maus has ar- i
rived and assumed his duties as inspector
general of this department, relieving |
Major Edward Field. Major Robert H.
Noble has been attending to the duties |
of the position since Major Field's illness. |
General Schwan, who has been ordered ;
to Manila and expects to sail on the City I
of Para, is in the city and is being enter
tained by Major Noble. General Schwan
commanded a division of General Miles'
army at the invasion of Porto Rico, and
since the war has been acting as assist
ant adjutant general.

Crane of the Twenty-fourth Infantry re

cruits being received at the Presidio since i

DISCUSSED TEMPEBANCE.
Rev. John A. B. Wilson Sharply Crit
icizes Supporters of Saloons.
In a discussion on "Temperance" by
the members of the Methodist Episcopal
Ministers' Union yesterday morning Rev.
; F. D. Bovard of Santa Rosa f*nd Rev.
! John A. B. Wilson of this city delivered
! forcible arguments on the subject. The
I former criticized the, attempt of Henry J.
Crocker to form a wine trust, which, he
! contended, would injure the cause of ter
n perance in his section. Dr. Wilson criti-
c ized the administration and condemned
| the canteen system. In part he said:
I have no controversy with my brethren on
the question of temperance. We all believe In
the prohibition of the liquor traffic upon prin
ciple. Our difference is upon the met.- 01
applying these principles. I take the position
that it is a great politic*! question as well
as a moral one. and it must have a great â 
political party in back of it. and no part> can
take it up because Issues make parlies, noi
parties make issues. ' , ..
The exeat issue of tariff has divided the
whole country into two hostile camps for years,
and yet the difference between the two bills _Oj
the various parties would not pay the drink mil
of the nation for two months. Twelve hundred
and fifty million dollars goes over the counters
of saloons every year for the debauchery of our
people. Nine-tenths of the crime is due to this
cause and 150.000 men are slain every year
through its evil effects. There is a hue and
cry and a national investigation because 01 |
the unsanitary camps in which 6000 of our sol
dier boys have died horrible deaths, but no j
investigation as to the ruin, dishonor de
bauchery and the final slaughter of the LAOtO
men through the drink question. And no man
In . politics .lares to fay one word in criticism
of the infamous traffic, from the- President of
the United States to the town Constable, fof i
the reason that the liquor men cast their votes
in "blocks" for the men who favor them in :
legislation and administration.
The diplomacy of the administration requires
that Protestant missionaries should not go to â 
Manila for their work, but has admitted 400
saloons to be opened in the city limits for the
debauchery of our soldier boys, who, respond
ing to their country's call, go out there in i
patriotic fervor, and who will come back to ua
ruined, not by the bullets of the Filipinos,
but by the sajoons supported by the adminis- ;
tration for their destruction.

"A GOLD QUARTER"â D. E. X., Plac
erville. Cal. There is no premium on what
is known as a "gold quarter." The United
States never coined gold quarters. Such
are tokens that were manufactured by
jewelers in the early days of California,
There are a great many in San Francisco,
where they are offered at 25 cents each.

Health officers. Dr. Kinyoun of the Ma
rine Hospital service last week ad
! dressed a letter to the board in which he
severely criticized the findings of Dr.
Barbat in the case of the two dead Jap

terday the board addressed a communica

ods were defended and his conclusions up-
After copious quotations from treatises

follows: that It was a well-known
; state that it was a well-known
fact to this board that there was a welt
equipped laboratory at the Federal Quar
antine Station. We have, been unable to

Officer has never yet been . sufflcientij

any report coming from this board was
willfully distorted and the facts withhe.d
from the Surveyor General of the Marine

teriologist to send you a slide of bubonic
plague bacilli, which was authentic, and

ROUTINE WORK.
Petitions and Objections Keep
the Supervisors Busy.
The Board of Supervisors met in regu
lar session yesterday afternoon. The May
or was absent and Supervisor Collins oc
cupied the chair. Regular routine busi
ness gave way to the reading of petitions
from many districts against the curtail
ment of the appropriation for the Fire De
partment. After this question was disposed
of the regular procedure was taken up.
The North End property owners, fearful
that the $1 tax limit will be rescinded, ad
dressed the following letter to the board:
To the Honorable Board of Supervisors-
Gentlemen: As taxpayers and voters of the
city, we hear with dismay the fact that
the proposed tax rate shall be $1.07. We
have reason to believe the members of your
honorable body will carry out and live up
to the solemn pledges you took when elect
ed to your offices. There is absolutely no
good reason why the tax rate should not
be 94 cents, as stated by the Assessor;
certainly not more than $1. Useless offi
cials, clerks who do nothing, large, fat con
tractors and rake-offs can be dispensed
with easily and without loss. Every on*
of the taS -eating officials should be re
stricted to what an ordinary business man
can easily run his businessâ fair amount
without provision for a lot of parasites
who eat out the substance of the munici
pal treasury.
The so-called St. Mary's Square scheme,
panhandle scheme and similar schemes for
filching the taxpayers should be abandon
ed and the schemers compelled to foot the
bills. The taxpayers should not be com
pelled to pay a lot of real estate agents
j who find themselves with a lot of unsal
able or unrentable property outrageous
prices for their holdings in order to in
. crease our tax-paying burdens by estab
lishing useless squares or panhandles. We
have enough squares, and Golden Gate Fork
end the Presidio afford all the recreation
ground and are even then only Infrequently
utilized, though we pay hundreds of thou
sands of dollars to maintain them. We
earnestly hope the board will strictly ad
here to the dollar limit without going bo
yond It for any special purpose whit-iver.
Let the tax rate be $1 or less for ill pur
poses, which, with the extraordinary State
rate, about (estimated) 58 cents, will be
sufficiently burdensome.
North End Property Owners' Association,
J. WILLIAMS. Secretary.
A petition "raying for the creation of a
free employment bureau to be maintained
by the city and county was referred to
the License and Ordinance Committee. It
was signed by several hundred of the
leading business houses of the city.
The Board of Appraisers appointed to
find out just how much the panhandle
scheme will cost submitted to the board
an estimate of the coin it will need during
the year to come and a request that it be
provided for in the tax levy. The sum re
quired is 115,000, of which 112,810 is to be
used "for salaries of members of board
and attaches," and another 51000 for "ad
ditional clerical assistance and incidental
expenses." The petition was referred to
the Finance Committee.
Supervisor E. 1.. Perrault introduced a
resolution that all supplies hereafter fur
nished any municipal department should
;be awarded by contract. The resolution
â  was killed.
I The surplus of $33,000 from the street
liKhtinsc fund was turnr-rt into the general
fund and the Treasurer ordered to make
the transfer.
A resolution was adopted which requires
an appropriation of $2i),ih'hj in the next tax
levy to pay the expenses incurred by the
Board of Engineers in the preparation of
plans and estimates for sewers and drain
afre.
The reception committee which was ap
pointed to prepare a welcome for the
First California presented its report, but
the matter was laid over a week.
