BAGGAGE OF
NIPPON MARU
IS RELEASED
Customs Authorities
Do Good Work.
ACTION TAKEN ONE DAY AHEAD
QUARANTINED PASSENGERS TO
BE RELEASED TO-DAY.
All Express Themselves as Highly
Plenscd With Their Courteous
Treatmentâ A Baby Girl
Born in Quarantine.
United States Surveyor of the Port
Chauncey M. St.. John took a force of Cus
tom-house inspectors, examiners, apprais
ers and laborers to the quarantine station
on Angel Island yesterday afternoon and
released the baggage of the passengers of
the Nippon Maru. who have been unwill
ing sojourners there for the past two
weeks. Early this morning the quaran
tine will be lifted by the Federal authori
ties and the grateful prisoners will be al
lowed to leave a spot whi'h will always
have rather unpleasant recollections for
them.
It was entirely owing to the courtesy of
the Custom-house officers that the bag
gage was examined to-day, and the work
was done expeditiously. But two hours
were consumed in handling a mass of
packages which covered the wharf, and
many were the encomiums passed upon thÂ«
officers for the creditable way in which
they performed their duties. Inspectors
of Immigration H. H. Scheil and A. de la
Torre were on hand, but had little to do.
The brunt of their labors will come to-
I day. when the steerage passengers are
released.
The people who have been sequestered
in the interest of public health were pro
fuse in their expressions of thanks to the
steamship company and to Dr. Klnyoun,
Federal quarantine officer, for their earn
est efforts to make their enforced stay on
the island as pleasant as possible under
the circumstances. The days have passed I
agreeably enough, and the courtesy of the
Custom-house authorities in passing the
baggage one day ahead was deeply ap
preciated by all concerned.
An event which served to add interest to
the isolation was the birth of a little girl
two days ago. The mother, Mrs. May
â Jackson Hill, was attended by Dr. Kin
youn and Mrs. 1 >r. Annie Walter Fearn,
one of the passengers. ;ind a medical mis
sionary. The mother is the wife of a
royal engineer in her Majesty's service in
the Orient. Considerable entertainment
was afforded in the naming of the baby, i
A request was made thai she be named
"Quarantina Angelina." but the mother;
compromised on Margaret Frances.
George Newhall and Thomas D. McKay,
Oriental passenger agent of a dozen lines,
were jubilant at their unexpected release,
and expressed themselves as highly
pleased at everything except the fact that ;
they were obliged to sleep In one room,
where there were thirteen iron beds, and :
they rather thought that the cubic air
law had been violated.
STRUCK WITH AN IRON BAR.
Hung Dung Chow, a Chinese Mer
chant. Attacked by Two High
binders.
Hung Purer Chow, a Chinese merchant
at 60S Jackson Btreet, swore to a com
plaint In Judge Mogan'a court yesterday
for 'he arrest of Lee Wee Too and an
other highbinder on the charge of as-
Bault with a deadly weapon.
The complaining witness has been in
terested in a civil suit for damages
against a Stockton boat, and yesterday
morning the two highbinders called at
his and demanded indemnity from
him. . When he refusc-i one of them
struck him on the head with an iron
bar, which he had concealed under his
blouse, knocking him Benseless. He had a
lump on hl.= head ns hiK ;>s a man's fist.
Given away with each cash want adver
tisement ordered in next Sunday's Call, a
magnificent portrait of Admiral Dewey,
printed in ten colors, size 14x21 inches,
ready for framing.
Mrs. Lung's Denial.
Mrs. Elizabeth Lung filed answer yes
terday to the complaint of her husband,
l>r. George A. Lung, surgeon of tho
cruiser Philadelphia, who seeks a divorce
on the grounds of Intemperance and
cruelty, in which she enters general denial
to the many allegations of the complaint.
She denii s that she has been guilty of in
tempeianee: that she treated her husb Li d
cruelly: that she was ever incapacitated
and unable to perform h<'r duties, ami
that she caused her husband great mental
suffering. She asks that a decree of di
vorce be denied her husband and that she
l <â > given Judgment for suitable alimony,
together with counsel fees and costs.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW y<"-i:k stock market.
NEW YORK July 10. â The continued tight
ness in the money market precipitated liquida
tion of the speculative accounts to-day wh!di
resulted in losses in value ranging from $1 to $3
a share in all the principal active stocks traded
In. Heavy selling: for London account marked
the opening en the Exchange, and as the day
progressed the successive calling of loans re
sulted in the raising of the loan, rate to 7 per
cent. This rate forced many speculators on the
loiig side to close out. There were important
points of weakness besides in one or two prom
inent stocks, of which the boars were quick to
take advantange. and which was a patent influ
ence in the tendency to decline. Most conspicu
ous of these was People's Gas. which opened
at 117. against 120 V on the last sale Saturday.
There were heavy offerings of this stock, but,
determined support worked a quick rally of
I\. Afterward it fell below the opening price
and closed with a net lass of nearly 3 points.
The motive of the selling was the reported
plan cf a rival company to organize a com
peting service upon the expiration of the pres
ent agreement. The demands made upon the
Metropolitan Street Railway Company on be
' half of its employes and the danger of a
costly str.ke caused the stock of that com
pany to drop ?A S at one lime. The dealings in
it v.ere not heavy and the tone was feverish
throughout, the last sale showing a net loss
or 2 1 -. All the local traction stocks were af
fectea sympathetically, Manhattan losing 3
points and Br.vkiyn Transit over 2 on the day.
Sugar was a.so notably weak, the selling being
credited to realizing by speculative holders.
The combined wt-aknes.s of these influential
eto< ks was sufficient to cause a demoralized i
feeling throughout the group of industrials. In
the railroad I.st the weakness was more re- j

LONDON. July 10.â There was thÂ» usual full

PROVISIONS.
Hams continue stiff and are still higher.
Lard is very firm. Bacon is higher In the East,
but has not advanced here, as the local
commission merchants are content to get
their profits on the former basis. Most of the
cured meats are coming from the East now, |
as the output of California has been materially 1
cut down by last year's drought.

The official report from the Consolidated Cali
fornia and Virginia mine for the past week
reads as follows: "The Comstock Pumping
Association has been engaged In the following
work at the C. and C. shaft during the pa.st
week; 1400 levelâ west drift has been ex
tended IS feet; total. 67S feet. The face is in
soft porphyry witlj seams of clay and occa
sional spots of quartz. The clay walls that
we have yet passed through are crushing the
timbers so that we had to relieve them during
the past week. We have to run 35 feet to
make connection with crosscut No. 2 from the
Con. Virginia shaft. 1650 levelâ Necessary re
pairs are being made on this level. 1750 levelâ
ThÂ« stopes have been extended one set south
and one set west, and in doing so we have ex
tracted 66 tons of ore of a gold value of $18
per ton. From the second floor we axe follow-
Ing: the clay wail to the southwest with a drift.
This clay "wall having shown spots of ore, we
will, as soon as possible, cut through it .and
see what Is beyond. On the sill floor at the
north end of the ttopes we ran a crosscut.
23 feet east; face in porphyry and quartz assay
ing Jo per ton. We have stopped this crosscut
and we are now sinking a winze on a hang-
Ing wall clay in this east crosscut. The winze
is now down rive feet in quartz, with streaks
of clay and porphyry and some ore. 1960 levelâ
The station on this level and 30 feet of the
south drift has been cleaned up, the face of
the later being in caved rock. We cannot tell
the condition of the drift beyond the cave.
We found It necessary to put In a small pump
to drain the seepage water out of this level,
said water being held back by the slime. Since
reaching the station we had occasion to make
but. two short runs with the pump, and for
the last twenty-four hours we have not run ,
the pump at all. We may, however, find a lit
tle water in the drift as we go south. All work
In the mine was suspended between the morn-
Ings of the 4th and sth. The machinery on the I
surface and underground is In good order and :
working nicely/

shaft. Central tunnelâ The joint Mexican east \

feet: face in porphyry. At this point the east

vein. The joint west crosscut will be started j

22 feet: total, 228 feet: face in hard han-ing
wall rock. The formation in this drift for 14

report, is clay and soft porphyry- The ground
will have to be retimbered. We are putting
in a switch and air pipe connections so that â¢
the vein may be prospected north . and south ;
as soon as the main west drift has been fid
vanced far enough not to interfere with the j
work. Work has been continued In the south- j
west drift and the drift extended 7 feet: total
length, 54 feet: face in quartz containing spots |
of ore. This vein, while improving as we fol
low it. is still irregular in form.

NOTE In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hind column nn.l the 1 successive tides M tr.Â»
day In the order of occurrence as to time. TJie
second time column gives the second tide or
the day, the third time column the third tide,
and the last or right hand column give* the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are- additions to the soundings on thÂ«
United States Coast Survey charts, except
â when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given la subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of trip lower low waters.

HER MIMING WAY
Yda Addis, the Once Brilliant
Writer, Believed to Be Now
Verging on Insanity.
Special Dispatch to ThÂ» Cull.
SANTA BARBARA, July 10.â Yda Addis
Ptorke, the once brilliant writer of stories,
chiefly relating to Mexican life. Is expect
ed to become a raving maniac at any mo
ment. She appeared in the Superior Court
this morning: to receive sentence on a
charge of writ and mailing libelous
letters to R. F. Winchester, and for which
a jury found her guilty. A large crowd
assembled to see how she would boar up
Tinder the prolonged strain. She came in,
being conducted by Sheriff Stewart, very
slowly, and plainly showed the effects of
the struggle she had on Saturday night
with Attorney Grant Jackson in an at
tempt to murder him. She was quite
lame and several times had to ask for
assistance.
When court was called Senator John J.
Boyce, her attorney, arose and said
that he had prepared a bill of exceptions
and a motion for a new trial, which he
would submit without argument. These,
he said, he had prepared with the aid of
his client, the defendant. Immediately
upon submitting the bill of exceptions and
motion for a new trial Boyce s&id.tha*
the action of the defendant since ihe tfjarf
had been such that as far as he vas per
serially concerned he would have nothing
further to do with the case, but that
hereafter Attorney Carrier, his associate,
would conduct the case. Carrier was ap
pointed to defend Mrs. Storke by the
court, while Boyce entered the case volun
tarily at a later date. Boyce conducted
the case himself, Carrier not being heard
at all. Senator Boyce'a decision came as
a surprise to the defendant, who al
though she has abused him on the street
for the result of her trial, hardly expected
him to desert her.
After a short conversation with Attor
ney Carrier, Mrs. Storke began: "If it
please the court, I will remain seated, as ]
1 am suffering too much pain to stand.
She went on with a. long argument In
well chosen words, principally to the ef
fect that she had secured further testi
mony which would prove beyond a doubt j
that F. N. Guiterrez was the author of
the libelous letters, and that in order to
corroborate this several reputable citizens,
although hosiilo to her, would swear to
affidavits implicating the said Guiterrez.
Senator Bovce arose at this time and
said that he 'was the only attorney that
had been consulted since the trial, and
that no such affidavits had been placed
in his hands.
Mrs. Storke said that she had informed
Boyce that she would swear to such affi
davits.
Judge Oster listened attentively to her,
and at the close stated he believed the
verdict a correct one, and thai the pen
alty for such offense to be utterly inade
quate. He then sentenced her to the full
jjeualtyâ one year In the County Jail.
Tho woman simply said: "I have vio
lated no law." She was then taken to
jail, and there began abusive talk about
her former attorney.
This afternoon Mrs. Storke was taken
to the Police Court for arraignment, un
df-r the charge of an attempt to murder
Grant Jackson. She appeared even
weaker than she had in the afternoon.
Judge Wheaton asked her to give her true
name, and she replied: Yda Addis
ritorke-Jackson. There may be sonic
doubt to the Jackson; but that will Lo :..;
issue in this case."
She contends that a contract "marriage
exists between her and Grant Jackson,
and that she visited his room yesterday
morning not with intent to murder and
commit ar^uult upon him, but as a right |
as his wifo.
Mrs=. Storke's examination will take
place Thursday, and she was required to I
give $suoo bail.
Two six-ehooters, a large bottle of
chloroform contained in a bottle stamped
"fiotica del Aguila Chihuahua Emillo
Tafon," a small bottle containing pruH.sic j
acid; a knife and some cord were taken to
the scene of the attempted murder by
her. Jt is believed by many that it was
her intention, to chloroform Jackson,
which she partially succeeded in doing,
and then mutilate him. After this she
undoubtedly intended to destroy her life
on the spot. This theory is borne out by
the fact ttiat everything necessary for I
carrying these things into execution were j
present; and also because she hovered
about the house all night long waiting for
an opportunity to enter, which she did at
daylight. She admitted to Jackson that |
she had been near the house all night,
stating that .she saw him enter rather
late, light the gas, read some time and
then retire.
Late this afternoon Mrs. Storke ap
peared to have become completely col
lapsed, but her physicians were sum
mon.- . in time to lend aid. It is believed
that the woman i.s about to become a rav
ing maniac. SI â  will allow any one to
interview her, and talks freely about her
case. !

HUSBANDS AND
WIVES MUST
BE PATIENT
Secret Trips to Reno
Are Delayed.
ARE LONGING FOR DIVORCES;
i
Unless Relief Be Speedily Given It Is
Feared That Some of the
Quarreling Couples May
Reunite.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, July 10.
While three-Quarters of the bench of
Alameda County Is rusticating and cool
ing off in some of the shady nooks of
the State many unhappy couples are |
forced to remain in the sweltering heat j
Of Oakland because there is no Judge on !
hand to attend to their petty domestic j
uuarrels.
Each day some unhappy husbands or i
wives linger around the courthouse in
the hope of obtaining the joyful informa- !
tion that the law has made them free i
from their hated partners, but Jud^e â 
Hall, having far more serious iaw busi
on hand, and being the only Judge :
i in attendance, has concluded that divorces j
ran wait until other matters are d:s- '
posed of.
Judge Ogden will pass on these family ;
1 quarrels when he returns, but as he only I
THE MORGUE CAUSES TROUBLE.
left for his vacation to-day, and as he is
entitled to a month, there is little bops
for theeÂ« Impatient husbands ana wives
who are forced to remain married against
their will and without their consent.
One of the most impatient of thia un
happy crowd is Mrs. Ida Strong, who
gave her testimony some weeks ago ana
who is now longing for a few strokes
from some Judge's pen to complete the
record. Her husband is in Just as much
of a hurry. Although there is a law for
bidding divorced couples to marry again
within a year in California, there are
all facilities for breaking the Intent 01 tnis
law at Reno or Carson.
Mrs. DeKay is another very anxious
woman who would like her decree, bne
has been married several years and her
husband baa played a matrimonial hide
and seek game. He contracted a naDlt ol
leaving her when he saw prospects of a
good income in the mines and of return
ing to share her income when he could
not do better. For the past two years he
has neglected to supply his little wife
with necessaries and she has tired of his
game. She brought suit for divorce two
months ago and everything Is in readiness
for the Judge's signature. Mrs. DeKuy
is anxious to have the matter concluded
as speedily as possible, for it is about
time, judging by the past, for her huh
band to return and spend a few months
as a star boarder and guest in the lodg
ing house which she is now running as a
means of support for herself and family.
There are several other divorce cases
waiting to be signed, and in one there If a
strong; probability that If it be not done
speedily the husband and wife will have
settled their differences and will be anxr
ious to have their case stricken off thÂ«Â»
calendar. '- ,
Walter Wright commenced suit to-oay
through his attorney. Darwin O. De Golia,
for a divorce from Chloe J. Wright on the
ground of desertion.

collided with another boy and received a
bump on the head, which caused him no
end of trouble and expense. He was af
flicted with epileptic fits periodically and j
medical treatment could do little to re- .
lieve him. At the age of 31 he decided
to have another operation performed and
he placed himself in the hands of the
Japanese physician. The operation was
successful and he no longer complains of
the epileptic attacks. Mr. Widman Is
well known to the residents of this city.
He Is a singer of ability and has ap
peared at entertainments given by fra
ternal organizations. He Is a member of
Borromean Council No. 129 Y. M. 1., and
other organizations. Friends will be glad
to learn of hiÂ» being in the land of the
living. â <â 'â â 
CHURCH AND
ROETH HEAR
A DEAL OF LAW
Astute Mr. Allen Is
Very Wary.
MORGUE NOW IN POLITICS
County Officials Hold a Session for
the Purpose of Playing Their
Old Game, "Pass the
Buck."
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, July 10.
Now it is the Morgue. Last week ft
was a flagpole. There Is always some
grave issue in local politics.
This morning Supervisor Roeth was to
have received In op< n session of the board
.-.. positive Statement fnrni District At
torney Allen regarding the establishment
of a County Morgue. Mr. Roeth has been
working a great deal In his mind on pub
lic morgues and recently he brought the
mutter up officially. He believes that one
half of the evils of the politics surround
ing the County Coroner will be banished
forever if there be a public morgue. The
District Attorney was ordered last week
to look up the law regarding the Coro
ner, the Morgue and its location. He did
so very fully and thoroughly and now
the Supervisors are just as wise aa they
wt-r.- a week ago regarding the point at
issue, although they are elaborately sup
plied With the law bearing on the sub
ject.
Mr. Allen, scenting the political element
In the agitation, refused to be enticed
into anybody's parlor, and, In the jargon
of the street, he determined to abide by
the letter of the Supervisors' request and
to remain on the fence as regards any
political Interpretation that might be de
rived from the statutes.
In his opinion he told the board that
they had a right to designate where the
office of Coroner shall be located, but the
law .says nothing regarding the location
of the Morgue. Sections and subdivisions
of statutes were quoted at great length
and the Supervisors were informed thai
there is a county official called the Coro
ner- that he must have his office at the
county seat: that the Supervisors must
erect -such public buildings as are neces
sary for the purposes of the county prov
ernment; that the law provides no office
hours for the Coroner, and that he must
summon not less than nine nor more than
fifteen iurors, etc., all of which the Su
pervisors have known since the days of
their political childhood.
Quoting from the wise derisions of the
Supreme Court, the District Attorney
read something about San Joaquin vs.
Budd, in which the august body says that,
it Is the duty of Supervisors to erect and
furnish a Courthouse and to provide ne
cessary offices for county officers, and to
designate the rooms they shall occupy.
Mr. Roeth listened patiently to the re
port but could not get. any satisfaction
from' it. He asked the District Attorney
the blunt question, "Has the board power
to determine the locality of the Morgue?"
"Gentlemen," said Mr. Allen, with his
softest smile, "I have given you the law
regarding your duties. It le not for me to
instruct you what to do."
"Have we the right to provide a pub
lic Morgue?" asked Roeth.
"1 have given yon the. law on the sub
ject " said Mr. Allen, with another smile,
"and I cannot presume to take to myself
your powers of judgment."
* Supervisor Will Church came to Rocth's
assistance and said the board had a right
to get just such an opinion as it desired
from the District Attorney, to which Mr.
Mitchell quickly replied that Mr. Allen
was there to quote the law, and that if
some members of the board wanted him
to sit ac a Supervisor they could elect him
to that office.

NATURAL GROWTH, NO BOOM
Fees of Becorder's Office for One Day
Beach Over One Hundred Dol
lars â Unprecedented in
County's History.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, July 10.
The County Recorder's office has broken
Its record. Not only was last month the
! busiest since January, 1895. but on last
Saturday there were more deeds, re
leases of mortgages and other papers filed
i than on any previous day in the county's
: history. The total fees for the day
' summed up $101 50. Exclusive of about
i ten or twelve gift deeds and deeds of
' trust, mortgages and reconveyances, the
following named tiled deeds of actual
bona fide sales:
G. H. Merrill to Ellen J. Baker, John
P. Beckett to John W. McKenzie, Ed
! Salz to James McKeown, Theodore Wag
! ner to Anna Gilbert, John Peterson to
Bernard Peterson, Charles J. Reuter to
j Phebe Leach, Central Pacific Railroad
Company to L. D. Inskeep, C. J. Fat to
Nellie S. Allen, James T. Moran to the
Roman Catholic Church, Mary E. Dewing
to W. J. O'Neill, Jessie E. Awppon to
Laura B. Bird, Homer A. Craight to J.
' Josephine Shoo, Henry Z. Jones to
Charles T. Carson, J. B. Draper to Emma
Levy, Mary C. Ironside to A. H. Doig, A.
L Stone to James B. Aver?. L. H. Dresco
to W J- Baker. J. E. Capwell to Edward
Metzger, Nettie F. Pickett to R. A Pick
ett H A. Pickett to E. D. Macdonal*,

ard W. Garrell to Jogepb H. Thompson,

Bigelow to Charles Itoeth, estate of

liam C. GfllM to M. Marks.
A large majority of all papers relate to
the great improvement that is gradually
taking place in North Oakland and other
parts of the territory recently annexed.
There is nothing in the nature of a
' "boom" about this steady progress, but
It Is a healthy development of a large
i area that has been held back for lack of
the facilities that are only to be ob
tained by a large municipality.
"Most of the property* that changed
hands in the deeds filed Saturday," said
Recorder Grimm's chief deputy, Robert
Leckie, to-day, "is in the district between
Grove street, San Pablo avenue, Tele
graph avenue and Piedmont. It is all
â¢ice property. Last month was the
highest for the number of deeds and
papers recorded since January, 1895. That
alone Is most wonderful, in view of the
fact thf.t the months of May, June and
July are supposed to be the most quiet
in "the year. The total receipts in our
office for June reached $19$n, while the
total fees for last Saturday was $101 dO.
This month will prove a stunner, 1 verily
believe.
"1 can hardly account for it. There has
been no boom. It is a kind of natural
growth and healthy increase in the real
estate market.
"It seems there has been a great deal
of picking up of cheap properties as well â
where people have been compelled to sell
fur various causes. Rut. withal, present
indications are most gratifying. Oh, Cali
fornia is the greatest changing place in
the world." continued Chief Deputy
Leckie, as he grinned gleefully over the
day's record.
GRACIE FRATES
FATALLY BURNED
OAKLAND. July 10.â Oracle Frates, 4
years of age, was fatally burned this
evening at her homo In San Leandro. The
little girl was playing on the front step
and had taken advantage of an opportu
nity to go Into the house and procure
some matchup. A few minutes later Grade
was In flamed. Her elder brother, who
was playing with her, tore the clothes off
her and" was himself badly burned.
The girl's screams attracted the mother,
who was in the rear of the house, and she
rushed to her assistance and helped ex
tinguish the flames. The child was fear
fully burned and flesh was falling from
many portions of her body. Dr. Tormey
was summoned and did everything possi
ble for the little sufferer, but he pro
nounced her Injuries fatal.

KISSING BUG SCARE
REACHES ALAMEDA
ALAMEDA. July 10.â John McCulley is laid up at his home, 1437 Cottage
street, suffering from fright. He believes that he was attacked by a
kissing bug Saturday night, and in his desperate fight to defend him
self from the poisonous bite of the famous insect he became exhausted
and loHt consciousness. When he regained his senses he was in the arms
of Policeman Ed Hadley, who was wondering what the trouble was with his
fellow officer.
The attack occurred at Morton-street station, where McCulley was wait-
Ing for a narrow-gauge train. McCulley says the bug was not quite as large
rs a blackbird and yet too big to be an Alameda mosqfUo. It flew into the
station nnd made straight for his face. All the kissing oug stories that have
appeared recently flaphed through the officer's mind, and visions of instant
death presented themselves to McCulley in rapid succession. His hands flew
about his head like a windmill In his efforts to ward off the bite of i.c bug,
and when he thought the kisser was about to get in an uppercut under his
guard he gave a yell and fell unconscious on the station floor.
Officer Hadley was attracted by the noise, and thought murder was being
committed. When McCulley regained consciousness the two policemen made
a search for the bug, but were unable to find it. McCulley insists that the
thing was black and unlike any insect he had ever seen before. He is not a
drinking man. He was so overcome by fright that he had to be taken to
hie home, and it may be several days before he Is able to resume hiÂ» duties.

WICKERSHAM WILL CONTEST.
Action Begun by the Disinherited
Son of the Late Banker.
SANTA ROSA, July 10.-Frank P.
Wickersham of Fresno, son of the late
banker, J. G. Wickersham, thin morning
commenced a contest of his father'B will
in the Superior Court. The estate was
valued at over $1,000,000. Under the. pro
visions of the will Frank Wickersham
was left only $5.
In the contest it is alleged that the de
cedent at the time and date of mak.ng
of the pretended will was incapable and
incompetent; that the pretended will is
not thf will of the late millionaire.
It is charged that Lizzie C. Wickersham
and Fred A. Wickersham, eon and daugh
ter of the deceased and brother and sis
ter of the contestant, exercised undue in
fluence over the decedent and persuaded
him to deprive contestant of said estate,
save the sum of $5.

Sergeant McCann Stabbed.
- Sergeant William McCann of the Third
Artillery got into a dispute with a large
number of soldiers at California and Du-
no.it streets late last night and was
stabbed in the back, arm, lei? and head.
He was picked up by a policeman and
removed to the Harbor Hospital. The
wounds are not serious. The assailants
ran way immediately after the cutting,
and up to an early hour this morning had
not been arrested.
Curious Custom.
In China it is the custom for guests at din-
ners to run around between the courses. This
Is supposed to keep the diner's digestion in,
good condition, but the nervous, hustling
American needs something else, and there la
nothing better than Hostetter's Stomach Bit-,
ters. If a man or woman is suffering with,
constipation, indigestion or any stomach
trouble, it's their fault if they don't get well.
Hostetter's Stomach Bitters will cure them.
See that a private Revenue Stamp covers the
nÂ»fk of the bottle.

RAILWAY COMMISSION.
Blackstock, Edson and Laumeister
Are Soon to Go to the Denver
Conference.
The Railroad Commission met yester
day. The matters considered were the re
plies received from the Valley road, the
California and Northwestern and the
The Transport Tartar, Chartered From the Canadian Pacific Railroad.
Santa Fe to communications that the
commission had previously addressed to
these roads, inquiring why they had not
adopted the new grain schedule that is
now in operation on the Southern Pacific.
As the replies showed that the old rates
in the district of the Valley road and
south of Tehachapi, the country tapped
by the Santa Fe, had not been changed
and as the California and Northwestern
is not a grain-carrying road and therefore
not affected by the new rate the replies
were merely read and placed on record,
as they called fur no action by the board.
A .communication from some meat com
panles acroM the bay, complaining of
transportation charges made by the
Southern Pacific, was answered by refer
rins the companies making the complaint
to the Southern Pacific, as the pattern
complained of did not come under the
jurisdiction of the commission.
In th.> early part of next month Com
missioners Blackstork, Kdson and Lau
meister will go East as far as Denver
to attend the annual convention of Rail
road Commissioners that is to be held
there While at the convention they will
endeavor to have next year's meeting held
in thi3 city.

ation and Banquet.
Magnolia Lodge, A. O. U. W., celebrated
the anniversary of Its Institution last
night and there was an unusually large
attendance of members. It was also in
stallation night and the following were
obligated for the current term by Dis
trict Deputy J. A. Cunnlnghan. assisted
by Past Masters A. W. Tuggy, J. Mi
chaels, C. Brind and D. D. Kidd: J. Bow
man, past master workman; W. P. Ja
coby, master workman; George P. Wet
more, foreman; Simon Cohen, overseer;
(' T Spencor, recorder; H. Stern, finan
cier 8. Kragen, receiver; J. Powers, in
side' watchman; J. A. Alexander, outside
watchman; M. H. Mohrdick, trustee.
AftÂ»Â»r the installation there was an ad
journment to a restaurant on O'Farrell
street, where the membership and in
vited guests parto,,k of an excellent sup
per in celebration of the anniversary.
Among the guests were Past Supreme
Master "Workman W. A. Jordan. Past
Grand Master Bahrs, Grand Recorder
Frank S. Poland and Grand Receiver Sam
Booth Pa.st Master Bowman was master
of ceremonies and after the supper he
called on Messrs. Jordan, Poland, Booth.
Cunningham. Tuggy and others to re
spond to toasts, and each did his best to
make the evening an enjoyable one. The
affair was got up by Past Masters M.
II Muhrdick, S. Isaacs, J. A. Cunning
ham H. Stein and C. T. Spencer, who
were the committee on entertainment.

TRANSPORT TARTAR ARRIVES
UNDER CHARTER FROM THE
CANADIAN PACIFIC.
Two Men Who Went Egg Poaching
Have Been on the North Far
allon Rocks for Nearly
Six Weeks.
The steamer Tartar, chartered by the
Government from the Canadian Pacific
Railroad, arrived in port late Saturday
night. She will be fitted out for a trans
port at the Government wharf and will
sail for Manila about the 20th inst. with
a regiment of regulars.
The Tartar was formerly on the run
between Southampton. England, and the
("ape of Good Hope, but when the Klon
dike boom was at its height the Canadian
Pacific people bought her for the Van
couver-Stickeen route. "When the bottom
fell out of the boom the railroad was left
with the steamer on their hands and noth
ing for her to do. She was then put upon
the Vancouver - Yokohama - Hongkong
route as an extra boat in the Empress
line, and made a number" of trips. When
the news was made public that I'ncle
Sam was very much in need of transports
to carry regulars to Manila and volun
teers home the Canadian Pacific sent an
agent to San Francisco, and aftor some
dickering succeeded in chartering the
Tartar to the Government. She was at
once sent to Nanaimo, B. C, to coal and
came down here in three days.
The Government will dispatch two
transports this weok. The Connemaugh
will get away this afternoon or to-morrow
with a lot of supplies for the army and a
deckload of cavalry horses. She will be
hurried back, as mounts for the cavalry
are urgently needed In the Philippines.
The City of Para will get away next
Thursday. Nearly all her freight is now
aboard, and Thursday morning the sol
diers assigned to her will go aboard. The
coffee season is now nearly over, so the
Mail Company can spare the vessel and in
consequence she will probably remain in
the Government service until all the vol
unteers have been brought home.
The Government officials and the people
generally have been looking for the trans
ports Ohio and Newport since Friday last.
The two steamers have the Second Ore
gon boys aboard, and the crowds who
want to welcome the soldiers have been
making the lives of the people in the Chief
Wharfinger's office miserable. About
every rive minutes the telephone bell rings
and when Chief Wharfinger Henderson
or Assistant Chief Wharfinger Dryden an
swers it the question almost invariably is,
"Have the transports with the Oregon
soldiers aboard been sighted yet?"
The chances are that both the Ohio and
Newport put into Nagasaki for coal, and
as The Call said before it will in conse
quence be to-morrow before they get in.
On their last voyage from Manila via
Nagasaki to San Francisco the Ohio was
twenty-eight and the Newport twenty
mi days making the run. To-morrow
both vcchcls will be twenty-eight days
out from Manila and until then they will
not be fully due. Of course if they did
not stop at Nagasaki they may pop in
at any moment From now on.
The Governor of Oregon does not ex
pect them before to-day. He will arrive
from Portland this morning and will ta-Ke
up his quarters at the Occidental Hotel.
Major Wilson, who recruited the regiment
In Oregon, is also here and he will go out
to meet the transports on the tug Gov
ernor lrwin with the Governor of Oregon,
Governor Gage and Mayor Phelan.
Everybody will know when the trans
ports are sighted, as the siren on tho
Eureka Planing Mills will blow for live
minutes without stopping. Again, when
the steamers are passing in through the
heads, the siren will take up her refrain
and will keep It up until the transports
have come to an anchor. The party on
the Governor lrwin â will then board tha
vessel that carries the headquarters of |
the regiment and welcome the boys back
In the names of Oregon and California.
The colliers Washtenaw and Progreso
had an interesting race down the coast. j
They left Puget Sound together and there |
was never more than a mile between them â¢
all the way down until the lightship was |
reached, when the Washtenaw made a j
spurt and beat the Progreso into port by !
about four miles. The Washtenaw came
from Tacoma and the Progreso from ;
Seattle. . , j
Wheat and barley are on the move and
in consequence several vessels have se- <
cured good charters. The Cypromene was ;
taken yesterday at 35s to load barley and
the Kenilworth and Ross-shire at 32s t:d .
to load wheat for England. The Hecla !
has been chartered' to load lumber on
Puget Sound for Australia.
H. Wood, an old man who keeps a cloth
ing store at 609 Clay street, was found al- !
most dead in bed from gas asphyxiation !
yesterday morning. He was hurried to
the Harbor Hospital, where Dr. Thomp- ;
son worked over him for quite a while :
and finally brought him back to life ;
again. The old man's sons pay it was
an attempt at sufcide, but the police seem
to think it was an accident. There have
been three deaths in the family of late !
and the old man has been very despond- j
ent. He was removed to the Lane Hos
pital during the afternoon.
About six weeks ago two men went out
to the North Farallon rocks (about eight
miles from the Farallon light) to poach :
sea fowl eggs. They landed on the rock
all right, but although two attempts have
since been made to take them off both j
have failed on account of the very rough â¢
weather. The men had enough provisions
to last them for some time and the eggs
would keep them from starving, but the
question is how could they exist without
water? Their supply must have given out
long ago and the chances are that they j
may have perished from thirst.
No one seems to know their names. I
They were both Telegraph Hill boyÂ» and. I
went out on the sloop Clara, Last week
the boat manned by Bob Spear and -Jack
Healy went out to the rock, but could
not get near enough to even hail the men.
It came on to blow and the men had to
run back to port. Another expedition
started out last night, and it is thought
that if th* men are still alive they will
be taken off and cared for.
PREPARING FOR APPEAL.
Pro Forma Judgment Sustaining the
Charter Entered by Judge
Seawell.
A pro forma judgment in favor of the
defendants in the action brought by Mar
tin Fragley against the Board of Election
Commissioners to test the validity of the ;
new charter was entered by Judge Sea- j
well yesterday morning. The judgmnt
waa entered upon an agreed statement of j
facts in order that a decision of the s>u- >
preme Court upon the issues involved ]
m Were b wÂ« .ome e argument preceding the ''
entering of judgment an the facts whteb
were to be included in the bill of excep
tions to the judgment, but finally it v-a*
agreed to enter all the facts which will
hereafter be found by the court *or the
purpose of the appeal. During tho heai
ing yesterday Deputy Registrar Rickarl
was put upon the stand to prove the U
uw)n which the proclamation for the
charter and the election of the Instru
ment were held. This over the agreement
stated was reached, and as soon as the
facts have been found the case will De
carried to the Supreme Court for de
cision.
Favors the Fire Department.
The Mission Business Men's Associa- ,
tion at a meeting held last night at Turn
Vereln Hall on Eighteenth street adopted
the following resolution regarding the
dollar limit and the Fire Department:
"Resolved, That the Mission Business j
Men's Association at a meeting held on
July 10, while appreciating the action of
the Supervisors in their desire to live up
to the ple-dge of keeping the taxes down ;
to the dollar limit, yet this association
deprecates what it terms a false economy â 
Of restricting the utility of the F'reDe
partment by shaving the appropriation to
*uch an extent as would cause with
drawal of several companies now in tlis
service; therefore be it
"Resolved, That we appeal to the 5U- i
pervlsora to make such an appropriation
as will permit the retention of all the
companies now In the department. rn<s
dissolution of any of the companies would ;
In the opinion of this association leave
the outside districts without amplÂ» pro- |
tection against fire and be the meeans of
subjecting property owners to double in- j
Burance rates."

WINTERS MUST HANG,
SUPREME COURT SAYS
GUILTY OF THE MURDER OF AN- i
DREWS AT BADEN.
His Hope Had Been xhat the Supe
rior Court Had ErredâRe
view of the Crime.
The Supreme Court has decided by the
close margin of four to three that Harry
Winters must hang for the murder of
Charles A. Andrews.
on November LT, 1897, C. H. Raymond
and Winters entered the Grand Hotel at
Baden, a village south of this city, with
burglarious intent, and were interrupted
in their work by Andrews, a boarder;
both robbers fired at Andrews; several I
shots took effect, and Andrews died with- j
in an hour. Raymond was arrested the
same night, was found guilty of man
slaughter, and was sentenced to impris
onment for life at Folsom. Winters wa3
arrested Borne time afterward; a Jury
I him guilty of murder in the Bret
degree, and he whs sentenced to he
hanged. The trial proved that it was
Winters' bullets that killed Andrews,
h-'iioo the lighter sentence of Raymond.
Winters was* taken to Folsom to be exe
cuted but his attorneys secured a wilt
robable cause from the Supreme
I Court, pending appeal, which stayed the
tJ The defendant claimed that the court
! erred in its instructions for the prosecu
tion, in that tb/jy were injurious to the de
fense and that the refusal ol the court
to admit an Instruction asked for by the
â  '.nit relative to th( law oi alibi, the
defense of Winters, was highly prejudi-
C1 justice Garoutte wrote the prevailing
opinion of the court, hoi, imp: that no error
had been committed by the court. Thi3
i was concurred in by Justices Harrison,
1 McFarland and Van Dyke. This decision
i upholds the lower court in refusing to
1 admit the instruction of the defendant rel-
I ative to his claim of alibi, in that the
word "reasonable" did not precede the
! word "doubt." Jii^i*''' Henshaw differed
I from this conclusion, maintaining that the
court erred in its refusal, and that the ab
sence of the word "reasonable" was rot
of so great moment as to exclude â¢ the
I Instruction, in that the entire Instruc
tion should be construed together instead
of being segregated. In this opinion he
was upheld by Justice Templo and Chief
Justices Beattv.
In cases where the decision is as close
as this a petition for rehearing is always
filed and the defendant will ask for a re
opening. If no rehearing Is granted the
renal ttitur will go down to the lower court

APPEAL TO WINEGROWERS.
i Paris Commissioners Issue an Inter
esting Circular Relative to Ex
hibits at Paris.
The Paris Commissioners are Just now
bendrog their energies to waking up the
winegrowers of the State to the oppor
tunity that is offered to gain a worldwide
reputation at the Paris Exposition. To
this end they have issued a circular an
nouncing that sufficient space nas been
allotted to have California wines exhib
ited as they have never been before. The
circular specifies every requirement of the
Commissioners.
The exhibit is to be of commercial wloes
and information Is required as to whether
or not the wine Is made from one grape
or blended; whether sold under the vine
' yard's name; of the roil In which the
grape was grown; of irrigation, sun ex
posure, annual productlpn, etc. Those
facts should be sent to W. H. alcNeil at
the Occidental Hotel. Photographic port
folios are to be made containing photo
, graphs illustrating California methods of
winemaking. â¢
The Commissioners have rented a ware
house on Mission near Now Montgomery
street and the work of gathering in the
i xhlblts will be rapidly pushed from now
on. _

CABRERA MAINTAINS
HIMSELF BY FORCE
GUATEMALAN DICTATOR BOTH
HATED AND FEARED.
An American Banker Exiled for Re
fusing 1 to Loan Money â Political
Prisoners Shot by Guards.
Mall advices from Guatemala confirm
the reports of an approaching crisis in
the Central American republic. That the
dissatisfaction will lead to a revolution
against the administration of President
Cabrera scorns hardly likely in view of
the fact that there is no money in the j
country to organize anything 1 approaching ;
a formidable insurrection. Uprisings In j
the cities are, however, expected, and
while they may not result In Cabrera's
downfall they will cause great loss to in
vested capital.
It is by no means unlikely that foreign
g-overnments. especially the Vnited States
and Germany and possibly Knglsuid, may
be called upon to interfere, if they have
not been requested to do so already.
f'abrera appears to be maintaining' him
self In office purely and simply by force.
Numerous tales of political- prisoners be
ing shot to death by their guards acting
under his orders are being circulated.
Among those said to have been murdered
in this manner by order of the dictator
are the following: Colonel Mateo Paz and
his brother Vincente, shot down while be
inp taken- to prison at Zacapa; Jose Ma
Ãbizo, assassinated by his guards on the
way to Chato: Don Hcrachio Trejo, in
San Marcos; Hoh Guadenrio Morales. In
! the Rancho Pan Augustin; Don Adrian
] Victoria, in Tecuhitan: Don Filadelfo
Pais, in Costa Cuca, and many others
whose names are unkown.
Five thousand refugees in Salvador and
as many more in Mexico and Honduras
are ready to take up arms against Ca
brera. The creat majority of the people !
of Guatemala detest him and would like
to see him deposed, but they also fear
him. The seriousness of the situation lies
in the fact that there is no money In the
country, and it is believed that a revo
lution, even if unsuccessful, would result
in hopeless national bankruptcy.
His Spite Costs Him Six Months.
James Johnson, who smashed two plate
glass windows in a store at 121 Montgom
ery avenue through spite because he had
been discharged by the company in which
the windows were insured, appeared for
sentence before Judge Conlan yesterday.
The Judge gave him six months in the
County Jail.

OF THE SEASON

Notes.
Society reserved its best showing for
"Lord and Lady Algy" and turned out
liberally in numbers and brilliantly in
costume at the Columbia Theater last
evening. Society on this occasion seemed
to know intuitively a gocxi thing before
seeing it. for "Lord and Lady Algy" is
not only a good thing In the way of
modern comedy, bnt it is one of the best
of the good things in that line that have
coma to Sun Francisco.
As the piece was presented at the
Columbia last night it was elaborately
staged and superbly 'costumed, the gowns
of the ladies In the fancy dress ball in the
second act being a shimmering dream of
beautiful fabrics. These scenic elabora
tions were the. framework for clever dia
logue, and funny situations that arose
naturally and glided into place without
effort. The act comes to an almost pa
thetic climax, when, after I^ord Alger
non, tipsy and almost maudlin, is or
dered out of the house by Brabazon Tud
way, Lady Algernon places her arms
about him and lovingly escorts him from
the room, loyal enough and womanly
enough not to desert, her husband in his
disgrace. , .
There was the stillness of intense mter
est in the crowded house as the curtain
rolled down upon the tableau, and then
the audience forgot its dignified reserve
and applauded again and again.
Henry Miller has a role that fits him.
He acts it with a naturalness and repres
sion as delightful as novel. The tipsy
scene, the most trying and risky of all.
because one so easily overdone, was i
played just to the limit and not a hair
breadth beyond.
Margaret Anglln's portrayal of the
good-hearted and volatile Lady Algernon
was artistic. H.-r naturalness and spon
taneity were refreshing. C. I>eslie Aliens
1-traba'znn Tudway was a good make up
for a caricature of Senator Mark Hanna
and was played conscientiouslyâ in spots,
and the ball room sen.- was not one of
the spots Th<-r<> he seemed to forget nis
earnestness and his Jealousy and wan
dered with long, stalky strides into the
land of burlesque. .
The comedy will live. It hns made the
hit of the seasonâ and of a very good sea
son, too.

An Infant Was Murdered.
Tn the case of the infant found dead in
the front garden of the house Xo. 1605
Oak street last Saturday, Autopsy Sur
geon Caglieri found that the child had
lived from ten to twelve hours after hirth
and had come to its death from exposure.
Coroner Hill h.'ld an inquest yesterday,
and the Jury returned a verdict that the
child came to Its death at the hands of
some party or parties unknown. The case
has been placed in the hands of the po
lice.
Minnie Adams' Trial.
The trial of Mrs. Minnie Adams,
charged with the murder of her infant
son John Richard Gray, is still under
way in Judge Lawlor's court. Yesterday
jurors, court and attaches left the court
room and visited the premises of the- al
leged murder. Aftr-r visiting the premises
the Jurors were dismissed, but wore in
structed to appear this morning that tno
bearing of t! â¢â  case might continue.
Mrs. Edmonds Had Heart Disease.
Dr. Cagrlieri, acting autopsy surgeon for
the Coroner, reported yesterday that Dr.
Mary E. Edmonds had come to her death
by valvular disease of the heart. â  Mrs.
Edmonds lived alone at 913 Bush street
and received a fatal shock last Sunday
night by the ringing of the burglar alarm
In her house.
